Gold Level criteria 
GL1 
The   project   aims   to   enhance   community   resilience   and   economic 
development by strengthening local business entities, implementing forest 
protection  measures,  and  managing  High  Conservation  Value  (HCV) 
areas   while   monitoring   environmental   impacts.   Through   awareness 
training on the consequences of illegal logging, the initiative will promote 
reforestation   and   the   maintenance   of   open   areas   while   fostering 
sustainable  livelihoods  such  as  fishing,  shrimp  farming,  crab  cultivation, 
mangrove   tea   production,   honey   harvesting,   and   ecotourism.   By 
integrating  these  efforts,  the  project  seeks  to  improve  the  community’s 
access  to  credit  facilities  for  environmental  conservation  activities  and 
enhance their Adaptability Vulnerability Index, directly impacting primary 
livelihoods and poverty levels. 
GL3 
Beyond  its  socio-economic  benefits,  the  project  plays  a  crucial  role  in 
conserving  the  region’s  rich  biodiversity,  which  includes  habitats  for 
critically  endangered  species  such  as  the  Proboscis  Monkey  (Nasalis 
larvatus),  Long-tailed  Macaque  (Macaca  fascicularis), and  Irrawaddy 
Dolphin  (Orcaella  brevirostris).  By  halting  timber  harvesting,  restoring 
degraded land, and promoting collaborative forest protection, the project 
not  only  safeguards  these  species  but  also  strengthens  the  ecological 
balance that underpins local livelihoods. Sustainable livelihood initiatives 
further reduce community reliance on environmentally harmful practices, 
fostering a harmonious relationship between conservation and economic 
stability.

1 SUMMARY OF PROJECT BENEFITS 
1.1 Unique Project Benefits 
The Kubu Raya Mangrove Conservation Project aims to protect and regenerate a unique 
inland wetland ecosystem hydrologically connected to the coastal mangrove system. It serves 
as a critical carbon sink and habitat for endangered species such as the Proboscis Monkey 
(Nasalis larvatus), Long-Tailed Macaque (Macaca fascicularis), and Irrawaddy Dolphin 
(Orcaella brevirostris). This project will: 
• Prevent deforestation and illegal logging by converting a logging concession into a 
conservation project 
• Enhance biodiversity by protecting critically endangered species and their habitats. 
• Generate socio-economic benefits by providing employment and sustainable livelihood 
alternatives. 
• Support Indonesia’s Nationally Determined Contributions (NDCs) under the Paris 
Agreement through measurable climate action. 
 
Outcome or impact estimated by the end of project lifetime

Dolphins (Orcaella brevirostris) that frequent the inland waterways. The project

species, many of which are listed as 'Endangered' by the IUCN.

Tanjung Beringin) have engaged in illegal logging for charcoal production, 
impacting carbon stock areas. The project will put a stop to these activities and 
instead provide employment opportunities as well as community-based 
sustainable commodities business models generation to support forest 
conservation-restoration and reduce deforestation drivers for mangrove 
ecosystem in PT. Kandelia Alam and the surrounding area.

2 PROJECT DETAILS 
2.1 Project Goals, Design and Long-Term Viability  
The project transitions a former logging concession into a protected conservation area in mangrove 
ecosystem, implementing Improved Forest Management (IFM), Wetland Restoration and 
Conservation (WRC), and Reducing Emissions from Deforestation and Degradation (REDD). 
• WRC activities: Conserve and restore degraded mangrove ecosystems by reforesting degraded 
areas with native mangrove species. 
• IFM Activities: Discontinuation of commercial logging and enhanced forest protection measures 
• REDD Activities: Monitoring and enforcement to prevent illegal logging and encroachment. 
2.1.1 Summary Description of the Project (VCS, 3.2, 3.6, 3.10, 3.11, 3.13, 3.14; CCB, G1.2) 
For  decades,  the  wetland  ecosystem  of  Kubu Raya,  West  Kalimantan,  operated  under a  long-term 
concession license for commercial timber harvesting, leading to gradual forest degradation and the 
loss of critical biodiversity. This project marks a fundamental shift by transforming the concession into 
a  conservation-focused  initiative,  integrating Wetland  Restoration  and  Conservation  (WRC) 
activities  with Improved  Forest  Management  (IFM) under  a Logged  to  Protected  Forest  (LtPF) 
framework.  In  addition,  the  project  implements Afforestation,  Reforestation,  and  Revegetation 
(ARR) as part of Wetland Restoration and Conservation (WRC) in degraded areas and enforces 
conservation  patrols  under Reducing  Emissions  from  Deforestation  and  Forest  Degradation 
(REDD) procedures to protect remaining forested areas. The concession covers a legally designated 
project boundary of 18,041.62 hectares, revised from its original 18,130 hectares following approval 
from the Ministry of Environment and Forestry. Within this area, 15,004.05 hectares were previously 
designated  for  commercial  harvesting,  1,266.71  hectares  remain  unmanaged  forests,  and 
1,678.25 hectares are occupied by inland water bodies, including rivers. While areas outside the 
designated  ARR  components  may  undergo  replanting,  only  degraded  areas  with  over  10  years  of 
degradation  history  qualify  for  net  GHG  emission  removals  per  VCS  methodologies.  The  project 
accounts for soil organic carbon (SOC) emissions across all three core components—IFM, ARR, 
and   REDD—ensuring   robust carbon   accounting.   Further   technical   details   on   the   applied 
methodologies can be found in Sections 3.1 and 3.2 of this PDD. 
 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
13 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.3 
 
Intact mangrove ecosystem in PT. Kandelia Alam that incorporates conservation-restoration efforts with local 
communities 
 
This conservation model is not just about protecting forests but also about securing the livelihoods of 
the  surrounding  communities.  While  no  permanent  settlements  exist  within  the  concession  itself, 
nearby villages such as Kubu and Batuampar have historically relied on these forests, often 
engaging  in  illegal  logging  and  unsustainable  fishing  practices due  to  economic  constraints. 
Recognizing  this,  the  project  incorporates  a "Beyond  Carbon"  business  model that  strengthens 
community-based economic alternatives, including sustainable fishing including mud crab and 
croaker fish, mangrove tea production, honey harvesting, and research-based ecotourism. To 
ensure the long-term viability of these ventures, the project will establish a sinking fund supported 
by  community-based  beyond  carbon  commodities,  which  will provide  financial  resources  for 
scaling community enterprises, improving infrastructure, and developing business cases that 
enhance  economic  resilience.  By  creating  sustainable  income  opportunities,  the  project  reduces 
pressure  on  forest  resources  while  fostering  long-term  environmental  stewardship  among  local 
communities. 
 
From an ecological perspective, the project safeguards a highly biodiverse landscape that serves 
as a critical habitat for endangered species, including the proboscis monkey (Nasalis larvatus), 
long-tailed  macaque  (Macaca  fascicularis), and  Irrawaddy  dolphin  (Orcaella  brevirostris).  By 
halting timber harvesting, restoring degraded lands, and enforcing conservation patrols, the initiative 
not  only  prevents  further  habitat  destruction  but  also  fosters species  population  recovery.  The 
project area and zone are not under any jurisdictional REDD+ program, ensuring that all conservation 
and emission reduction activities remain independent and community driven. 
 
The project is expected to generate significant GHG emission reductions and removals, with a total 
of 77,990,668 tCO2e over the 30-year crediting period, equating to an annual reduction and removal 
of 2,599,688 tCO2e, excluding emission from the activity caused by project program, leakage, and 
risk buffer adjustments. These reductions stem from avoided emissions through IFM, WRC, and 
REDD activities and direct carbon sequestration via ARR efforts. 
 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
14 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.3 
Ultimately, this project represents a holistic approach to climate action, biodiversity conservation, 
and sustainable development. By leveraging carbon finance not only to protect forests but also to 
support local economies, it creates a scalable model where environmental and economic resilience 
go hand in hand.

13
  
Agriculture, forestry, and other land use 
Project activity type 
1. WRC:  in  the  entire  project  area  since  the  project  area  is 
mostly mangrove forest ecosystem and swamp forest. 
2. IFM: in   forest   areas   legally   approved   for   commercial 
harvesting/logging where  timber  harvesting  activities  are 
discontinued, 
3. ARR:  in  unmanaged forest and  degraded (in  10  years or 
earlier prior  to  project start  date) areas, where  replanting 
activities will be implemented, this activity is part of the WRC 
activity, and  
4. REDD: in other unmanaged forest areas in the project area 
(for at least 10 years from project start date) under threat of 
deforestation   and   degradation   from   illegal   logging   and 
encroachment that will be prevented by physical and socio-
economic interventions. 
 
2.1.4 Project Eligibility (VCS, 3.1, 3.6, 3.8, 3.18, 4.1; CCB Program Rules, 4.2.4, 4.6.4) 
This project comprises four distinct AFOLU activity components in VCS Scope 14 grouping 
described in VCS Standard (version 4.7) in sections 3.1, and 3.2.1 and 3.2.8. These four

 See Appendix 1 of the VCS Standard 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
15 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.3 
activity components are IFM, WRC, ARR and REDD and are described in sections 2.1.1 and 
2.1.3 of this PD. The projects are planned to be continued for at least 60 years based on 
Indonesian forestry regulations which confirms requirements stated in section 3.2.11 of VCS 
Standard (version 4.7). 
 
The PD is to be submitted for VCS pipeline listing together with other supporting information 
specified  in  VCS  Registration  and  Issuance  Process  (version  4.6)  and  VCS  Standard 
(version 4.7). The pipeline listing is required to be completed with 3 years from project start 
date as per section 3.8.2. However, due to uncertainty regarding VCS participation related 
guidance by the Indonesian National Carbon Registry (SRN), the PP could not submit the 
draft PD to Verra for pipeline listing, and hence this process has been delayed. 
 
This PD is submitted for VCS pipeline listing and start of validation. 
 
The VCS methodology choices applied to the project for the three types of VCS project 
activity components are explained in section 3.1.1 of this PD. For the IFM component, the 
VCS  methodology  VM0011  (version  1.1)  is  applied,  and for  the WRC, ARR  and  REDD 
components, the relevant modules of VCS methodology framework VM0007 (version 1.8) 
has been applied.

2.1.9 Project Ownership (VCS, 3.2, 3.7, 3.10; CCB, G5.8) 
Kandelia Alam, the project owner, has secured the rights to manage the natural forest area from the 
Ministry  of  Environment  and  Forestry.  With  clearly  defined  boundaries  and  an  approved  ten-year 
management plan, with the following milestones: 
 
- Environmental Impact Assessment (AMDAL): Approved by the Governor of West Kalimantan 
province (Decree No. 660.1/1808/X/DLHESD-B, dated 29 October 2007). 
 
- Concession   Management   Permit: Granted   by   the   Ministry   of   Forestry   (Decree   No. 
SK.249/Menhut-II/2008, dated 24 June 2008).

SK.295/MENLHK/SETJEN/HPL.1/3/2023, dated 31 March 2023.

Ministry of Environment and Forestry (Decree No. SK.8311/2024, dated 4 June 2024).  
 
The original copies of the business licenses and regulatory approvals, that are business confidential 
information, are available for verification by the VVB at the time of VCS project validation.

2.1.12 Differences in Assessment/Project Crediting Periods (CCB, G1.9) 
Based  on  the  decision  to  discontinue  the  commercial  harvesting  of  timber  with  reference  to  the 
previous  business  license  awarded  to  a  previous  owner  of  the Project  Proponent’s company, and 
subsequently  the  new  owner’s  applying  for  a  new  business  license  for  undertaking  eco-system 
conservation activities, both the project start date, and the crediting period start date are considered 
same, which is 1 January 2023. 
 
The  necessary  assessments  were  completed  prior  to  completion  of  the  PD  for  submission  to  the 
Indonesian  National  Carbon  Registry for  local  approval  and  registration for  GHG  emissions 
accounting, climate adaptive capacity and resilience, community, and/or biodiversity. 
 
Hence, there is no difference in assessment/project crediting periods.

2.1.14 Physical Parameters (CCB, G1.3) 
The  project  area  encompasses  an inland estuarine  mangrove ecosystem,  covering  approximately 
18,041.62  hectares (refer  to Figure  1, Figure  2 and  Figure 3) This  area  includes  a  network  of 
navigable inland waterways, tributaries, and canals connected to the Kapuas River, which flows into 
the Java Sea. The project area spans the coordinates of latitudes 0°31'34.71"S to 0°39'59.89"S and 
longitudes 109°21'48.01"E to 109°41'10.14"E. 
 
The overall locational layout of the project area is provided in Figure 2 delineating the areas where 
the  three  types  of  VCS  project  activities  will  be  implemented.  The  immediate neighbouring areas 
around the project boundary include: 
 
- North: Plantation forest concession and oil palm plantation concession 
 
- East: Plantation forest concession 
 
- South: Protected forest (Hutan Lindung or HL) and limited production forest (Hutan Produksi 
Terbatas or HPT) 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
21 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.3 
 
- West: Forest areas categorized as limited production forest (Hutan Produksi Terbatas or HPT). 
 
Figure 1: Project area location and general features 
 
 
Figure 2: Planned locations for implementation of project activities 
 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
22 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.3 
 
Figure 3: Areas for WRC, IFM, ARR and REDD project activities 
 
PT Kandelia Alam, which is one of the Project Proponents, manages the forest area under a 
10-year management plan (RKUPH), with the latest plan approved for the period of 2022 until 
2031.  This  plan  outlines  various  forest  management  activities  approved  by  the  Ministry  of 
Environment  and  Forestry,  covering  a  total  project  area  of  18,041.62  hectares,  where  PT 
Kandelia  Alam  works  hand  in  hand with  surrounding  communities  to  establish  several 
partnership model towards a holistic landscape approach. In relation to the RKUPH, the total 
project  area is  sub-divided  with  respect  to  type  of  activities  to  be implemented  and  the 
landcover in the project area (refer Table 10). 
 
Topography. The land is relatively flat of elevation in the range 0-500 m with slope less than 
3% as per the Biomass Assessment Report (or IKHB – Inventarisasi Karbon Hutan Berkala). 
Most of the area is completely flat with height between 0-3m range above mean sea level and 
is influenced by periodic high tides. 
 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template

Soil Type 
The  project  area  is  predominantly  composed  of  mineral  soils,  covering `15,803  hectares 
(96.57% of the total land area of ~16,363 hectares) with the remaining area comprising shallow 
organic swamp soil. A study by WWF Indonesia
16
 found that the soil mainly consists of deep, 
heavy clay, which is firm in most locations but loose near rivers and creeks.

The area does not have any arable agricultural activities; however, palm oil plantations are located on

The Biomass   Assessment   (IKHB)   Report completed   in 2023   provides information   on   soil 
characteristics  based  on a  field  survey conducted  by  the project  proponent in  the  project  area. 
Besides biomass assessment, this survey included soil survey for soil texture based on guidelines by 
the   Department   of   Agriculture   and   other   official   references   (Departemen   Pertanian,   1992; 
Poerwowidodo,  1992).  The  results show  that in the sample plots in the ‘Periodic Comprehensive 
Forest  Inventory Report  (or IHMB as  per Indonesian acronym)’ used in  this survey, contained soil 
with 87.25% clayey loam, 7.24% sandy loam, and in the remaining 5.51% a mixture of sandy loam, 
loamy sand, and loam shown in Table 9.

Climate. Located in a wet tropical climate zone, the project area receives an average monthly rainfall 
of  274  mm,  with  an  annual  range between  889.2  mm  and  3,288  mm,  as  noted  in  the  Biomass 
Assessment  Report  2023.  The  highest  average  rainfall  occurs in  June  (423 mm)  and  the  lowest  in 
February (74.1 mm). 
 
On average, there are 19 rainy days per year, with December having the highest number (24), followed 
by November (23). Monthly humidity ranges from 83% to 90% at the Pontianak meteorological station 
and  from  73%  to  84%  at  the  Siantan  station,  with  average wind  speeds  of  5.1  knots/hour  and  3.5 
knots/hour, respectively. 
 
Temperatures at the Pontianak station range from 23.3°C to 32.4°C, with an average high of 33.2°C, 
while at the Siantan station, temperatures range from 24.1°C to 31.1°C, with an average of 26.9°C. 
 
Hydrology. According to the 2007 Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (or AMDAL as per 
Indonesian  acronym),  the  project  area  lies  within  the  Keluang  Watershed  Area,  encompassing  the 
Radak,  Sepada,  Cupi,  and  Sapar rivers and  their  tributaries.  Water  levels  in  these  rivers  fluctuate 
significantly  between  rainy  and  dry  seasons,  rising  to  approximately five meters  during  the  rainy 
season  and  dropping  below  1.5  meters  during  the  dry  season,  with  some  tributaries  drying  up 
completely.  The  moderate  flow  speed  of  these  rivers  minimizes  erosion  on  the  riverbanks  and 
riverbeds but increases sedimentation, particularly in estuary areas. 
 
River depths, ranging from two to five meters, support water transportation using local boats ('klotok' 
and  'ketinting')  and  serve  as  sources  for  fishing  and  other  commercial  activities.  Despite  the 
fluctuations, the surface water levels and discharge rates remain relatively stable throughout the year. 
Water  quality  in  rivers  passing  through  residential  areas  remains  good,  supporting  activities  like 
bathing, washing, and hygiene (MCK/Mandi Cuci Kakus). 
 
Types of vegetation. The project area of approximately 18,041 hectares includes: 
• Forested areas: 11,171 hectares, 
• Non-forest, and degraded cleared areas: 5,192 hectares, and 
• Rivers and waterbodies: 1,678 hectares. 
 
According  to  the  2021  land  cover  map,  the  project  area  consists  of  secondary  mangrove  forest, 
secondary swamp forest, secondary dryland forest, shrubs, swamp shrubs, open land, camp areas, 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template

of the total project area). The land cover profile is summarized in Table 10.

2.1.15 Social Parameters (VCS, 3.18; CCB, G1.3) 
The project area is in Kubu Raya Regency, West Kalimantan province, but administratively covering 
3 districts connected to each other by water passage. Although there are no settlements within the 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
27 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.3 
project boundary,  three  villages  are located nearby in  the  project  zone,  all  within  the  Kubu  Raya 
Regency administrative area. 
 
The three closest  villages  to  the  project  area,  all  within  30  kilometers, are  assessed  for  social 
parameters that are described below. 
 
 
Figure 6: Map of villages around the project area/boundary in project zone 
 
1. Kubu village 
Kubu village,  part  of  the  Kubu sub-district in  Kubu  Raya regency, in West Kalimantan  province of 
Indonesia, covers an area of 23,408 Ha. The village's economy thrives on agriculture, fisheries, and 
plantations. The local weather sees average temperatures ranging from 20°C to 34°C. 
 
Located  along  the Kapuas  River, Kubu  village relies  heavily  on  river  transport,  which  presents 
significant challenges due to higher costs compared to land transportation. 
 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
28 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.3 
 
Figure 7: Map of Kubu village 
 
Kubu village is rich in natural resources, including mangrove forests, agricultural lands, plantations, 
and rivers. The local community utilizes these forest resources for both timber and non-timber products 
to  sustain  their  economy,  while  others  depend  on  agricultural land  and  plantations.  During  certain 
seasons,  the  community  engages  in  additional  activities  to  fill  their  unproductive  time.  Key  insights 
from focus group discussions with the Kubu village community include: 
 
• Fisheries: Fishermen experience higher catches of crabs and snapper from August to December, 
thanks to favorable weather conditions that make fishing more productive. 
 
• Logging: Weather  conditions  have  minimal  impact  on  logging  activities,  except  when  stable 
sea/river water levels make log transportation more challenging. 
 
• Charcoal  Production: This  activity  relies  on  logging  for  raw  materials  and is  carried  out  year-
round, regardless of weather conditions or market prices. Production typically increases during the 
dry season compared to the rainy season.

CCB v3.0, VCS v4.3

Table 13: Historical events in Kubu village 
No. Year Key events 
1. 1732 
The origins of the Kubu Kingdom trace back to an expedition 
of 45 Arab explorers from the Hadramaut region in Yemen, 
who were tasked by their teacher to spread Islamic Sharia 
across the eastern seas of Asia. Among these explorers was 
Syarif Idrus Al-Idrus, who played a pivotal role in establishing 
the village. 
2. 1768 According to local folklore, the name "Kubu" is derived from 
the fortifications built during the Dutch colonial period in 1778 
by Sultan Syarif Al-Idrus, the first king of the Kubu Sultanate. 
Sultan Syarif Al-Idrus not only founded Kubu village but also 
served as its inaugural ruler. 
3. 1772 All people agreed to appoint Syarif Idrus as their leader. 
Under his leadership, the community saw significant 
advancements in defense, economy, and trade. 
4. 1775 Syarif Idrus was crowned as the first king and was given the 
title "Tuan Besar Raja Kubu." The original royal palace was 
later transformed into what is now the Grand Mosque. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
30 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.3 
No. Year Key events 
5. 1910 In 1910, the Dutch East Indies colonial government 
established the Bestuur Committee to oversee the Kubu 
Sultanate. Syarif Kasimin, a relative of the Kubu Sultanate, 
was appointed by the Dutch to lead this committee, with 
assistance from Syarif Shaleh, a loyal servant of the colonial 
administration. 
6. 1958 The Kubu Sultanate eventually integrated into the Unitary 
State of the Republic of Indonesia (NKRI). Kubu village 
subsequently became the capital of Kubu Subdistrict in 
Pontianak Regency, West Kalimantan province. 
7. 2007 On August 10, 2007, Kubu Subdistrict was officially 
transformed into Kubu Raya Regency through the enactment 
of Law No. 35 of 2007.

females.  The  total  population  based  on age-group  is included  in Table 14,  which  shows  a  higher 
proportion of relatively younger individuals.

2. Batu Ampar village 
Batu Ampar is located in Batu Ampar sub-district, Kubu Raya regency of West Kalimantan province in 
Indonesia, covering an area of 67,297 hectares. The village's name has a local origin dating back to 
the 1940s during the Japanese colonialism. At that time, a large rock obstructed the main road. The 
Japanese army used dynamite to blast the rock, scattering the rock into pieces. The local people then 
named the village "Batu Ampar" that corresponds with "scattered rock". 
 
Batu Ampar  village is  rich  in  natural  resources,  including  mangrove  forests,  agricultural  lands, 
plantations, and rivers. Approximately 70% of the villagers rely on forest resources, both timber and 
non-timber,  to  support  their  livelihoods.  The others primarily depend  on agricultural  and  plantation 
lands,  but  the  assessment  shows  that most  of  Batu  Ampar communities  owns  the  land  for farming 
rather than workers. During certain seasons, villagers engage in various activities to supplement their 
income. Some insights collected from the Batu Ampar village community are summarized. 
 
• Fishermen's Activities: The months of June, July, and August see an abundance of fish, crabs, 
shrimp,  snapper,  semeran,  and  ketang. Favourable weather  conditions  during  these  months 
contribute to higher catches. 
 
• Logging Activities: Weather conditions have minimal impact on logging, though stable sea and 
river levels are crucial for easier transportation of logs. 
 
• Charcoal Production: This activity is closely tied to logging, as it relies on raw materials from the 
forest. Charcoal production continues year-round, irrespective of weather or market prices, though 
it tends to increase during the dry season. 
 
• Supplementary Income Sources: Women in the village often engage in roofing and nipah palm 
craftsmanship,  primarily  producing  items  upon  request.  Nipah  roofs  are  commonly  used  as 
construction materials for charcoal ovens. 
 
• Honey Cultivation: Moar Honey and Kelulut Honey are less popular businesses, with only 2-6 
villagers actively involved. Kelulut honey production peaks between May and August. 
 
• Women's  Role: Women  significantly  contribute  to  the  local  economy,  engaging  in  various 
businesses except for fishing (snapper, semeran, and ketang) and timber harvesting.

The  history  of  Tanjung  Beringin village  dates  back  to a  royal  marriage  between  the  Kubu  King's 
daughter  and  King  Labai's  son.  As  a  wedding  gift  for  their  children,  the  Kubu  King  granted  a  new 
territory to King Labai. In this area, there was a large banyan tree (beringin) with a trunk so wide that 
it stretched across the Mendawak river. This tree was located on a bay (tanjung), leading the local 
people to call the area Tanjung Beringin, meaning Banyan Bay.

2.1.16 Project Zone Map and Project Location (VCS, 3.11, 3.18; CCB, G1.4-7, G1.13, CM1.2, 
B1.2) 
The project area along with the project zone are identified in Figure 10. The project zone is defined 
as the area encompassing the project area(s) in which project activities that directly affect land and 
associated  resources,  including  activities  such  as  those  related  to  provision of  alternate  livelihoods 
and community development, are implemented. The boundaries of the project area comprise area(s) 
where project activities will be implemented with the aim to generate net climate benefits as well as 
community and bio-diversity benefits. 
 
As described earlier in this PD, the project zone includes two villages where some individuals in the 
communities depend on forest resources for their livelihoods. In Kubu (part of the Kubu sub-district in 
Kubu Raya regency of West Kalimantan province) and Batu Ampar (part of the Batu Ampar sub-district 
in  Kubu  Raya  regency  of  West  Kalimantan  province),  some  villagers  engage  in  illegal  logging  for 
firewood and charcoal production, while others rely on mangrove forests for mudcrab and fish fishing. 
Recognizing the need for viable alternatives, the project will introduce several community development 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
40 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.3 
interventions focused on sustainable commodities, particularly mudcrab sustainable fisheries through 
climate-positive fisheries improvement program (C
+
FIP)
17
, and research-based ecotourism. 
 
 
Mudcrab fisheries are one of the sustainable alternative livelihoods that PT. Kandelia Alam champions along with local 
communities, not only non-extractive, but also reduce the pressure towards mangrove ecosystem. 
 
By developing (C
+
FIP) mud crab interventions as a structured, sustainable livelihood, the project will 
offer an economically viable alternative to mangrove deforestation, reducing reliance on destructive 
harvesting practices.  
 
Additionally, research-based ecotourism will be promoted as a long-term economic driver, showcasing 
the region’s rich biodiversity while fostering conservation awareness. These initiatives will not only 
diversify  income  sources for local  communities but also  reduce  pressure on  the  broader mangrove 
ecosystem  beyond  the  project  scope. We  will  conduct  capacity  building  and  education  for  local 
communities to enable them to become local ecotourism operators. By shifting behaviors away from 
extractive  practices, the  program  strengthens  climate  resilience  and  fosters  a  more  sustainable 
relationship between local livelihoods and environmental conservation. 
 
The  concession boundary  for  the  project  area  was  updated  by  the  Ministry of  Environment  and 
Forestry in  2023, and  within  the project  area  the net  climate  benefit  will  be  focused.  Additionally,  it 
serves as a crucial biodiversity conservation area, home to key species and diverse flora and fauna. 
The  project  is  committed  to  enhancing  these  habitats,  contributing  significantly  to  large-scale 
biodiversity conservation. 
 
 
17
 C
+
FIP is an initiative to develop community-based fisheries supply chain and sustainable best practices to ensure their climate 
resiliency, the climate-positive addition is to ensure that any sustainable commodities produced will have positive impact towards 
climate, since it was sourced from a conservation-restoration area. This will provide leverage for each commodity to tap into the 
potential of premium pricing and targeting specific market that would signify responsible sourcing it its supply chain.

2.1.17 Project Activities and Theory of Change (VCS, 3.6; CCB, G1.8) 
This  is  an  ecosystem conservation  project in  an  inland  wetland  system with four main  objectives, 
namely,  (1)  conservation  of  native  flora  and  fauna  that  are  unique  to  this  eco-system,  (2)  thereby 
contributing to global climate change mitigation by removing GHGs from the atmosphere and reducing 
GHG  emissions and  local  climate  adaptation  by  retaining  natural  protections  against  erosion  and 
floods from rising sea levels, and (3) improving health conditions for communities who reside in the 
project zone but get impacted from charcoal-making activities with illegally logged timber harvested 
by some individuals in their communities that lead to deforestation and forest degradation activities. 
Thus, the overall project will involve activities to protect the native biodiversity in addition to protecting 
and  rehabilitating  the  nature  forests  and  vegetation and  (4) developing  community-led,  resource-
responsible  business  models  to  support  alternative  livelihoods.  By  bringing  together  markets  and 
consumers that  value  products  that  are sourced  and  handled  responsibly,  this  project  will  increase 
income. This is one strategy to reduce pressure on extractive practices including charcoal making from 
mangrove deforestation. 
 
 
Integrated patrol (incl. drone patrol) between PT Kandelia Alam and local community in mangrove protection, mainly 
from illegal loggers & illegal fishers. 
 
 
Aligned with the above objectives, these WRC’ project activities being and to be implemented include 
conserving  existing  biodiversity  to  prevent  extinction  and  promoting  sustainable  use  of  natural 
resources,  supporting the  local  economic  development, improving health  and  sanitation, improving 
capacities for local institution development, and women empowerment. Community development is a 
core component, so that alternate livelihood practices are undertaken with an aim to address socio-
economic  dependencies  on  time to  prevent illegally  sourced timber  exploitation from  unmanaged 
forest. These will involve raising community awareness and build capacities to enhance their quality 
of life through alternative livelihood generation while preventing drivers of deforestation and climate 
change causes. The key WRC project activity components for undertaking the above are spread over 
certain areas within the project boundary as below: 
 
- CIW + IFM: 15,004.05 hectares 
- RWE + ARR: 464 ha (out of total area of 655 ha being re-planted) 
- CIW + REDD (AUDD): 1,266.71 hectares 
- CIW Stand-Alone: 161 hectares 
 
Based  on  intended  conservation  actions  proposed  by  the  Project Proponents to  the  Indonesian 
government, the approval for the most recent forest utilization business work plan (or ‘RKU’) dated 
04/06/2024 provides  for  implementation  of  the  forest  conservation  and  re-planting  activities  for  the 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4

period 2022 to 2031 to  replace  previously  approved  commercial  logging  of  mangrove  and  swamp 
forests while re-growing unmanaged degraded forests with native endemic species and conserving 
the unmanaged forests from illegal human induced deforestation and degradation. With reference to 
this  RKU  and  as  explained in  the ‘Theory  of  Change’ below,  the  project  activity  sub-areas  are 
demarcated in Table 26.

2.1.18 Sustainable Development Contributions (VCS, 3.17) 
Indonesia is a signatory to the ‘UN Sustainable Development Goals (“UN-SDGs”)’ and has several 
national  policies  and  priorities  to  achieve  those  goals.  Several  direct  and  other indirect  sustainable 
development benefits that this VCS project is expected to deliver are contributory to the sustainable 
development policies. These are under the umbrella of the Indonesian state philosophy which is the 
Pancasila, or the Five Principles. 
 
The project is expected to contribute to UN-SDGs that the government aims to address, such as the 
following: 
 
Goal 1: End poverty in all its forms everywhere 
Goal 2: End hunger, achieve food security and improved nutrition and promote sustainable agriculture 
Goal 5: Achieve gender equality and empower all women and girls 
Goal 6: Ensure availability and sustainable management of water and sanitation for all

Benefits to the Climate Change Mitigation 
The project is expected to generate significant GHG emission reductions and removals, with a total of 
77,990,668.69 tCO2e over the 30-year crediting period, equating to an annual reduction and removal 
of 2,599,688.96 tCO2e, excluding emission from the activity caused by project program, leakage, and 
risk buffer adjustments. The sale of verified carbon units (VCUs) will provide financial sustainability for 
the project. 
 
Concurrently,  pre-project  activities  such  as  illegal  logging  for  charcoal-making  by  non-native 
communities  are  expected  to  be  fully  halted.  This  will  be  reinforced  through  integrated  community 
collaboration periodic patrols, ensuring ongoing forest protection while fostering local participation in 
conservation  efforts.  By  engaging  local  communities  in  monitoring  and  enforcement,  the  project 
strengthens environmental stewardship and further safeguards the mangrove ecosystem from future 
degradation. 
 
Benefits to the Climate Change Adaptation 
The  project  activities  are designed  to  prevent  land degradation  and  soil  erosion  by  conserving  and 
regenerating native forests, particularly mangroves, which are among the most effective coastal tree 
species  for  stabilizing  shorelines  and  mitigating  sea-level  rise.  By  restoring  and  protecting  these 
ecosystems, the project enhances natural coastal defenses, reducing the vulnerability of surrounding 
communities to climate-induced flooding and extreme weather events. 
 
While direct monitoring of all accrued adaptation benefits may not be feasible, proxy indicators—such 
as  reduced  deforestation  and  forest  degradation,  cessation  of  harvesting,  natural  regrowth,  and 
replanting—serve  as  measurable  signals  of  climate  resilience  improvements.  These  restored 
mangrove  forests  also  function  as  vital  nursery  habitats  for  fish  and  crustaceans,  supporting  local 
fisheries,  enhancing  food  security,  and  sustaining  biodiversity  critical  to  the  region’s  ecological 
balance. 
 
To ensure the long-term viability of these adaptation measures, the project incorporates sustainable 
financing mechanisms, including the Sinking Fund
19
, which provides a structured financial model to 
scale  up  community-led  climate  adaptation  initiatives.  By  linking sinking  fund  mechanism to 
sustainable livelihoods such as ecotourism and fisheries, the project ensures that local communities 
directly benefit from conservation efforts, securing both economic resilience and high-quality seafood 
commodities due to a thriving mangrove ecosystem. 
 
Benefits to the sustenance of water quality 
The water quality in the rivers, canals, and internal waterways within the project area has been found 
to be suitable for human use, as noted earlier in this PD. Critical aquatic fauna, including the Irrawaddy 
dolphin, thrive in these waters due to their suitability for sustaining marine life. 
 
The project’s conservation efforts will prevent land degradation and soil erosion by protecting and 
regenerating native mangrove forests, which play a crucial role in filtering sediments, reducing runoff 
pollution,  and  maintaining  water  clarity.  By  preserving  these  natural  filtration  systems,  the  project 
enhances overall water quality, ensuring healthier aquatic ecosystems that support fish populations, 
crustaceans, and other biodiversity-dependent livelihoods. Improved water quality will further benefit 
local fisheries and coastal communities, reinforcing the ecological and economic sustainability of the 
region. 
 
 
 
19
 A sinking fund, generated from commodity sales profits, will drive the sustainable growth of community-led businesses 
independent of ongoing project operations. This fund acts as a catalyst for development by supporting collective activities like 
capacity building, equipment acquisition, and data traceability. To guarantee proper management, an external professional fund 
manager will be appointed, ensuring effective and professional oversight

CCB v3.0, VCS v4.3 
Socio-economic benefits 
The discontinuation of commercial harvesting has led to a rise in illegal logging within the project area, 
often involving casual laborers from surrounding villages rather than native communities. Recognizing 
the  need  for  sustainable  alternatives,  the  project  is  committed  to  providing  equal  employment 
opportunities and fostering collaborative partnerships to ensure long-term community resilience and 
forest conservation. 
 
To  ensure  accountability  and  impact  assessment,  the  project  will  monitor  employment  data  and 
benefits, tracking how these interventions contribute to economic stability, reduced illegal logging, and 
improved  environmental  stewardship.  By  integrating  livelihood  development  with  conservation,  the 
project  strengthens  both  social  and  ecological  resilience,  making  climate  action  beneficial  for  both 
people and nature. 
 
Beyond  direct  employment,  the  project  will  support surrounding local  communities  through  diverse 
alternative  livelihood  interventions,  including:  (i)  Fisheries  Development – Enhancing  sustainable 
fishery practices and mudcrab cultivation, ensuring economic security while reducing pressure on the 
mangrove  ecosystem,  (ii)  Research-Based  Ecotourism – Creating  community-led  tourism  models 
centered  around  the  region’s  biodiversity,  such  as  Irrawaddy  dolphins,  proboscis  monkeys,  and 
mangrove forests, offering both employment and environmental education opportunities, (iii) Business 
Model  Development – Facilitating  small  enterprises  in  mangrove-based  products  (e.g.,  honey 
harvesting, mangrove tea production) and other nature-based industries, (iv) Strategic Partnerships – 
Working with academic institutions, NGOs, and private sector actors to scale up training, investment, 
and access to markets for community-driven enterprises. 
 
Other  benefits  include  access  to  education  on  conservation,  institutions,  decision-making,  financial 
management  and  business  development.  They  can  also  learn  from  other  local  communities  both 
nationally  and  internationally  who  have  been  successful  in  managing  businesses  by  maintaining 
sustainable natural resources.  
 
Benefits to women and children 
The charcoal-making processes are generally operated by women in village households, who often 
have no choice but to bring their children to their place of work. The reduction of charcoal production 
will not only eliminate the smoke and hazardous conditions that women and children are continually 
exposed to but also result in significant health benefits, reducing the risk of respiratory diseases and 
long-term illnesses. 
 
Beyond the health benefits, the project’s sustainable business models, particularly in fisheries and 
research-based  ecotourism,  will  provide  alternative  income  opportunities  for women,  reducing  their 
reliance  on  charcoal-making.  Women  can  engage  in  value-added  fisheries  activities  such  as 
processing  and  selling  fish  and  mud crab  products,  as  well  as  participate  in  ecotourism  services, 
including  hospitality,  guiding,  and  artisanal  crafts.  These  opportunities  will  empower  women 
economically, enhancing their financial independence while improving household stability. 
 
Furthermore, by reducing the need for women to work in hazardous environments, children will have 
a greater opportunity to focus on education rather than being exposed to unsafe work conditions. As 
families transition to sustainable livelihoods, the well-being, education, and future prospects of children 
in  these  communities will  also  be  significantly improved. While  it  is  not  practical  to  fully  assess  the 
direct  long-term  health  and  socioeconomic  benefits,  it  is  expected  that  the  elimination  of  charcoal-
related hazards and the introduction of alternative income sources will foster a safer, healthier, and 
more sustainable future for women, children, and the broader community.

Women  and  youth  as  part  of  the  community  will  be  actively  involved  in our program  so  that  their 
aspiration and involvement for conservation, business development and monitoring activities will be 
integrated.

2.1.21 Benefit Permanence (CCB, G1.11) 
 
The  Project  Proponents  have  designed  this  initiative  to  be  self-sustaining,  ensuring  its  long-term 
viability  through  a  combination  of  community  engagement,  sustainable  business  development, 
ecosystem restoration, and regulatory enforcement. By integrating conservation efforts with economic 
opportunities, the project fosters long-lasting incentives for local communities to protect and restore 
their environment. 
 
Empowering Communities Through Sustainable Livelihoods 
 
A  key  pillar  of  the  project’s  permanence  strategy  is  providing  alternative  livelihoods  that  reduce 
dependency on destructive forest-based activities. To achieve this, the project promotes community-
led business models that create sustainable income while strengthening environmental stewardship. 
These initiatives include: 
 
• Sustainable fisheries – Supporting capture fisheries and enhanced cage aquaculture for mussels, 
shrimp, and mudcrabs, offering a viable alternative to destructive fishing and reducing pressure 
on mangrove ecosystems. 
• Research-based ecotourism – Establishing eco-education programs, nature-based tourism, and 
guided  mangrove  experiences, leveraging  the  region’s  biodiversity  to  generate  income  while 
promoting conservation. 
• Non-Timber  Forest  Products  (NTFPs) – Encouraging  honey  production,  mangrove  tea,  and 
mangrove batik, allowing communities to benefit economically without depleting forest resources. 
• Seafood  processing – Enhancing  the  value  of  locally  harvested  seafood  through  improved 
processing techniques, increasing market access and economic returns. 
To support these initiatives, the project includes training programs that improve sustainable resource 
management,  enhance  production  techniques,  and  help  communities  establish  viable  enterprises. 
These  training  efforts  ensure  that  local  residents  can  transition  away  from  unsustainable  practices 
while securing long-term economic stability. 
 
Community Enterprises and Sustainable Financing 
 
Beyond supporting individual livelihoods, the project fosters community-led enterprises that facilitate 
access to financial institutions and market linkages, ensuring long-term economic resilience. Central 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
51 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.3 
to this effort is the Sinking Fund, an innovative financing mechanism designed to incubate and scale 
local businesses while maintaining strong conservation incentives. 
 
Funded by verified carbon unit (VCU) revenues, this financial model will support: 
 
• Enterprise   development   for   sustainable   fisheries,   ecotourism,   and   other   community-led 
businesses 
• Ongoing conservation efforts to protect and restore mangroves 
• Capacity building for local entrepreneurs to access markets and funding 
This approach ensures that economic benefits continue even after the initial project phase, reinforcing 
long-term conservation and sustainable development goals. 
 
Conservation, Habitat Restoration, and Forest Protection 
 
The project actively restores degraded and deforested areas, planting native mangrove species with 
direct  participation  from  local  communities.  These  efforts  revitalize  critical  ecosystems,  enhance 
climate resilience, and prevent coastal erosion—critical benefits in the face of rising sea levels. 
 
To  safeguard  these  restored  habitats,  the  project  integrates  community-based  forest  protection 
programs,  ensuring  local  engagement  in  preventing  illegal  logging  and  land  encroachment.  By 
including  integrated  community  collaboration  periodic  patrols,  the  project  enhances  enforcement, 
further ensuring long-term conservation outcomes. 
 
Regulatory Support and Long-Term Vision 
 
The Project Proponents have secured regulatory approval and work closely with local authorities and 
community leaders to enforce conservation measures. Additionally, the project’s long-term viability is 
supported  by  a  30-year  crediting  period,  with  the  potential  for  a  30-year  extension,  aligning  with 
Indonesian   regulations   to   establish   a   60-year   framework   for   conservation   and   community 
development. 
 
During  this  period,  the  project  allows  non-destructive  traditional  activities,  such  as  sustainable 
harvesting  of  non-timber  forest  products  (NTFPs),  ensuring  that  communities  retain  economic 
opportunities without harming the ecosystem. 
 
By securing strong financial backing, regulatory support, and community involvement, the project is 
positioned to successfully transition into a lasting conservation model. Moreover, it provides a blueprint 
for  similar  conservation  projects  in  Indonesia  and  beyond,  demonstrating  that  climate  action  and 
community development can go hand in hand. 
 
 
PT. Kandelia Alam develops its own nursery ground in our own  camp using natural propagule collections to ensure 
native species restoration and at the same time reduce the cost of restoration (seed purchase)

2.1.22 Financial Sustainability (CCB, G1.12) 
The funding for this project implementation and sustenance over the project lifetime is secured by the 
Project Proponents from own equity contribution and other forms of private support, as stated above 
in  section  2.1.21.  The  information  is  commercially  sensitive  and  will  be  shown  to  the  VVB  during 
validation  event  without  publishing  in  any  public  database. Landscape  portfolio  blended  financing

Fund as stated above, this will be separated from the project operation financing. 
2.2 Without-project Land Use Scenario and Additionality  
Without the project, commercial logging and illegal deforestation would continue, leading to: 
• Increased carbon emissions from deforestation. 
• Loss of critical habitats for endangered species. 
• Soil degradation and increased vulnerability to climate change. 
• Community displacement due to resource depletion. 
2.2.1 Conditions Prior to Project Initiation and Land Use Scenarios without the Project (VCS, 
3.13; CCB, G2.1) 
Since 2008, PT. Kandelia Alam has held a license from the Ministry of Forestry to harvest timber for 
commercial  purposes on  a  20-year rotation cycle involving  trees  with  minimum  10  diameter. This 
permit is valid until 2053. 
 
Due to economic reasons, since 1990, some community members residing in villages in the project 
zone but outside the project area also started illegally logging within and around the project area to 
produce mangrove charcoal. Furthermore, this activity has been passed down through generation in 
the villages adjacent to PT. Kandelia Alam’s mangrove ecosystem. This unauthorized activity, which 
lacks legal management rights, has led to environmental degradation due to non-compliant logging 
methods, leaving the native forests degraded and lands abandoned. Such practices have also been 
referred to in several published information sources. 
 
It is to be noted that Indonesia boasts the largest mangrove coverage in the world, accounting for 21% 
of the global mangrove area. According to the FAO
20
, this amounts to 3.1 million hectares, while the 
Ministry of Environment and Forestry
21
 reports it as 3.31 million hectares. In West Kalimantan province 
alone,  mangroves  cover  approximately  161,967  hectares.  The  government  identify  and  map 
mangrove  areas  for  various  interest  such  as  indicative  targets  for  mangrove ‘Forest  and  Land 
Rehabilitation (“RHL”)’, mangrove ecosystem boundaries for zoning plans, determining the potential 
for  ecotourism,  tsunami  hazard  safety  studies,  studies  of  the  country's  outer  boundaries,  carbon 
calculations, research and education, as well as fisheries potential. 
 
In early 2022, under new management, PT. Kandelia Alam proposed a revised management plan to 
the Ministry of Environment and Forestry for a 10-year management plan (2022-2031) to discontinue 
timber harvesting in the inland wetland and, instead, implement the following: 
 
• Ecosystem conservation: in  areas  planned  for  logging  and  those  under  threat  of  or  already 
subjected to illegal logging,  
 
• Replanting in degraded areas: Due to illegal logging in several areas (including protected areas 
not designated for logging). 
 
20
 FAO. 2020. Global Forest Resources Assessment 2020. Main report. Rome. https://doi.org/10.4060/ca9825en 
21
 Kementerian Lingkungan Hidup dan Kehutanan. 2021. Peta Mangrove Nasional. Direktorat Konservasi Tanah dan Air. Ditjen 
PDASRH. Jakarta. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
53 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.3 
 
Since  January  2022, the  infrastructure,  equipment,  and  nursery  have  been  developed  and  since 
January 2023 conservation and restoration activities have been progressively implemented. The new 
management decided to implement ecosystem conservation and replanting efforts, by implementing 
involving four types of project activities: 
 
• Improved Forest Management (IFM): for areas with discontinued timber harvesting. 
 
• Wetland  Conservation  and  Restoration  WRC: for  all  areas  by  periodic  patrol  internally  and 
collaborative   action   with   local   stakeholders   including Afforestation,   Reforestation   and 
Revegetation (ARR): for areas with the implementation of replanting activities; some re-planting 
areas are not accounted for ARR. 
 
• Reducing  Emissions  from  Deforestation  and  Forest  Degradation  (REDD): for  areas  with 
illegal   logging   and   destructive   fishing   prevention   through   physical   and   socio-economic 
interventions, and 
 
These  components  aim  to  stop  timber  harvesting  from  planned  and  unplanned  deforestation  and 
degradation, complemented by replanting in degraded areas along with the social interventions with 
local communities. These activities help prevent the loss of Above-Ground Biomass (AGB), Below-
Ground Biomass (BGB), and Soil Organic Carbon (SOC). 
 
Without  the  implementation  of  the  project's four components, the  project  area  would  likely  face 
continued and  progressive  deforestation  and  degradation.  This  expectation  is  based  on  pre-project 
and baseline scenarios detailed in the Project Description (PD), specifically in Section 2.1.17, which 
outlines the Theory of Change. 
 
2.2.2 Most-Likely Scenario Justification (CCB, G2.1) 
Several sources of information were reviewed to determine the most-likely scenarios corresponding 
to each of the three components in the project. The information sources include the following: 
• Satellite imageries  procured  and  analysed  in  time-series  covering  the  project  area and project 
zone; 
• Observations made  in  the  project  area  and  project  zone  and  documented  in  photographs  and 
videos; 
• Formal (including  FPIC)  and  informal  communications  including  FPIC with  communities  in  the 
project zone residing in the Kubu and Batu Ampar villages; and 
• Interviews/discussions  with  former  management  and  employees  of  PT  Kandelia  Alam  who  the 
Project Proponents have replaced. 
Detailed justification of the most likely land use scenario in the baseline is provided in section 3.1.4. 
 
2.2.3 Community and Biodiversity Additionality (CCB, G2.2) 
While existing regulations in Indonesia, at national and local levels, require implementation of project 
activities following the principles of sustainable development. This VCS project, by addressing forest 
and ecosystem conservation needs, is also assisting with conservation of the native biodiversity that 
would not have happened in its absence. 
 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
54 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.3 
The illegal deforestation activities that have continued from the past involved some of the residents 
from the communities in the project zone together with the illegal timber logging business. However, 
those activities are unsustainable and did not provide steady and sustained income to the participating 
residents. Given the remoteness of the project location, without employing sufficient resources and 
supporting logistics, it is challenging to prevent negative impacts on the biodiversity in the project area 
and by association impacts on the communities in the surrounding areas. The use of timber products 
collected  and  use  in  charcoal  making  processes  in  the  villages  are  hazardous  for  women,  elderly 
residents  and  children  when  exposed  to  fumes  and  smoke  for long  periods  of  time. The  Project 
Proponents  have  offered  to  provide  equal  employment  opportunities  and  collaborative  partnership 
models  through  community-led  business  model  generation  for  those  individuals  to  participate  on  a 
long-term and sustained basis in the implementation and operations of the VCS projects.  
 
The project aims to transform community livelihoods and conserve biodiversity, creating sustainable 
alternatives  for  illegal  loggers  and  fishermen  engaged  in  destructive  practices.  Our  comprehensive 
plan includes: 
 
• Develop  community-led  business  model  and  enterprise  to  support  sustainable livelihoods: 
Providing  alternative  income  sources  such  as  sustainable  fisheries,  ecotourism,  and  the 
production of  value-added  products  like  fish,  shrimp,  crabs, honey, and mangrove  tea. To  then 
connect  local  products  with  markets  and  financial  institutions,  fostering  economic  growth  and 
stability. 
 
PT. Kandelia Alam working collaboratively with local fishers (mudcrabs and croaker fish) to establish beyond carbon 
value chain through conservation premium price commodities to support local communities and sinking fund 
development for scalability. 
 
• Ecotourism development: In  Kubu  and  Batu  Ampar,  many  villagers  historically  depended  on 
illegal logging for firewood and charcoal or relied on mangrove forests for fishing and crabbing. 
To  provide  sustainable  alternatives,  the  project  promotes  mudcrab  aquaculture  and  research-
based  ecotourism  as  viable  livelihoods,  reducing  pressure  on  the  mangrove  ecosystem  while 
fostering economic  resilience.  A  key  example  is  the  "Kubu  Raya Estuary  Cruise 2024," led by 
Tanjungpura  University’s  Marine  Science  Program,  in  collaboration  with  Nusantara  Climate 
Initiative - PT  Kandelia  Alam,  Blue  Forests,  and  the  Batu  Ampar  community.  Held  in  October 
2024,  this  initiative  combined  mangrove  conservation  education,  hands-on  monitoring,  and 
community  engagement,  including  a  storytelling  session  by  Suheri,  a  former  logger  turned 
conservation  advocate.  These  efforts  align  with  the  project’s  broader  ecotourism  strategy, 
leveraging the region’s rich biodiversity—including  proboscis  monkeys,  long-tailed  macaques, 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
55 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.3 
and Irrawaddy dolphins—to attract responsible tourism. With Pontianak Airport just an hour away, 
ecotourism presents a scalable, sustainable economic model that enhances conservation while 
benefiting local communities. 
 
Kubu Raya Estuary Cruise 2024," led by Tanjungpura University’s Marine Science Program, in collaboration with 
Nusantara Climate Initiative - PT Kandelia Alam, Blue Forests, and the Batu Ampar community 
 
• Development of Sinking Fund: A sinking fund, generated from commodity sales profits, will drive 
the  sustainable  growth of  community-led businesses  independent of  ongoing  project  operations. 
This fund acts as a catalyst for development by supporting collective activities like capacity building, 
equipment  acquisition,  and  data  traceability.  To  guarantee  proper  management,  an  external 
professional fund manager will be appointed, ensuring effective and professional oversight 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
56 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.3 
 
 
Without  these  initiatives,  pressure  on  the  habitats  of  endangered  species  such  as  the  proboscis 
monkeys, long-tailed macaques, and Irrawaddy dolphins would continue to increase, threatening their 
survival.  These  species  are  iconic  in  West  Kalimantan  and  Indonesia,  making  their  conservation 
critical. 
 
The Project  Proponent holds  a  forest  management  business  permit  (PBPH)  from  the  Ministry  of 
Environment and Forestry, allowing the sustainable management of the mangrove forest. Previously, 
timber  harvesting  was  conducted  under  this  permit,  but  current  management  has  ceased  these 
operations as part of a ten-year management plan. This shift significantly reduces forest degradation 
and  helps  maintain  the  existing  mangrove  ecosystem.  The  project  will  also  restore  illegally  logged 
areas,  improving  habitats  for key  species  of proboscis  monkeys  (Nasalis  larvatus),  long  tailed 
macaque (Macaca fascicularis), and Irrawady dolphins (Orcaella brevirostris). 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
57 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.3 
 
Long-tailed Macaque, Proboscis monkey, and Irrawady dolphin. 
 
The Project Proponent's measures will also address gender inclusion, education, hygiene, and health 
status for mothers and children, the elderly, and women, as highlighted in the SDGs (Section 2.1.18.). 
All  efforts  are  based  on  thorough  information  gathering  from  affected  communities  and  groups, 
ensuring that all initiatives meet the actual needs of the community. 
 
Despite  national  and  local  regulations  in  Indonesia  mandating  sustainable  development,  previous 
management  could  not  halt  illegal  logging, leading  to  deforestation  and  biodiversity  loss.  This VCS 
project addresses these issues, ensuring the conservation of native biodiversity that would otherwise 
be at risk. 
 
Together with  other socio-economic  project  activities  mentioned  in  this  PD,  the Project  Proponents 
have created sustainable development opportunities for the communities in the project zone hoping 
that the implementation of a holistic landscape management can be achieved. By focusing on forest 
and ecosystem conservation, the project supports the sustainable development goals and preserves 
the unique fauna and flora of the region. 
 
None of these will be possible if the VCS project is not implemented, thereby demonstrating that this 
project  is  additional  with  respect  to  any  community  and  biodiversity  matters as  we  are  using  the 
blended financing approach including climate financing from VCS. 
 
2.2.4 Benefits to be used as Offsets (CCB, G2.2) 
Please refer to sections 4 and 5 in this PD for community and biodiversity benefits. However, none of 
these benefits are planned to be used as offsets by the Project Proponents.

2.3 Safeguards and Stakeholder Engagement   
• Engagement with local communities through employment, capacity building, and alternative 
livelihoods. 
• Indigenous community inclusion, ensuring traditional knowledge is integrated into conservation 
efforts. 
• Environmental monitoring and compliance, adhering to international best practices. 
2.3.1 Stakeholder Identification (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB G1.5) 
The stakeholder identification is carried out through the following: 
 
• Relationship developed by Project Proponent in their previous mangrove forest management.  
o Through regular meeting with the community 
o Through community development program (PMDH) 
 
• ‘Rural Rapid Assessment (“RRA”)’ conducted in Kubu village (19 October 2023) and Batu Ampar 
village (26 June 2024). The RRA manages to identify: 
o Community leaders 
o Institution analysis and its influence 
o List of issues in each village 
 
Public Consultation for submission of the DRAM (Dokumen Rencana Aksi dan Mitigasi or Action and 
Mitigation Plan Document) to the Indonesia National Registry in Pontianak was held on 7 December 
2023. 
 
The  local  stakeholders  identified  are  related  to  communities  identified  in  section  2.1.15 of  this  PD. 
However,  the  implementation  and  sustenance  of  the  proposed  VCS  project  is  not  dependent  on 
involving residents and representative of every such village/settlement/hamlet other than those who 
are: 
 
• directly or indirectly involved in illegal logging and associated deforestation and degradation activities 
in the project area as a source of income from sale of timber, 
• depend on timber extracted from the project area, both as legally harvestable (in the past) as per the 
previous RKU (i.e., business license for harvesting) and illegally removed, and 
• individuals and groups who are suppliers (and prospective) of services and supplies to the Project 
Proponent(s) for activities within the project area and surrounding villages. 
 
A social survey/consultation process comprising the approach of a ‘Free and Prior Informed Consent 
(“FPIC”)’ was designed and conducted,  and  ongoing  monitoring  is  in  progress.  This  process  was 
designed with reference to and in compliance with the relevant Indonesian government regulations for 
community consultations in forestry sector activities. These include: 
 
• Regulation of the Minister of Forestry of the Republic of Indonesia Number P.39/Menhut-II/2013 
concerning Empowerment Community Through Partnership 
• C169 Indigenous and Tribal Peoples Convention, ILO, 1989 
• Convention on Biological Diversity, United Nations, 1992 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
59 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.3 
• ‘Convention on International Trade in Endangered Species of Wild Fauna and Flora (“CITES”)’, 
signed at Washington DC, 1973 and amended at Bonn, 1979, and 
• ITTO/IUCN Guideline for the Conservation and Sustainable Use of Biodiversity in Tropical Timber 
Production Forests, ITTO – IUCN, 2009. 
 
A ‘Standard Operating Procedure (“SOP”)’ was prepared by the Project  Proponents to  conduct  the 
FPIC process in a structured manner as relevant to the needs of this VCS project. In additional to the 
above,  other  regulations  and  guidelines  that  were  referred  to  are  identified  in  the  SOP.  Additional 
information is included in section 2.3 of this VCS PD. 
 
Since 2023,  the Project  Proponents have  also  initiated  various  types  of ‘Community Partnership’ 
programmes  with  the  communities,  which  is  a  continuation  from  social  initiatives  that Project 
Proponent initiated few years earlier based a ‘Village Participative Study’ mentioned earlier in section 
2.1.15 of this VCS PD. 
 
These Community Partnership Program activities include: 
 
1. Support in sustainable agriculture, livestock, and fisheries 
a. Assistance with vegetable seeds and fertilizers package towards community-based agroforestry 
program 
b. Planting/Maintenance of vegetables package 
c. Sedentary farming/paddy rice, honeybees, and sustainable fisheries support 
d. Connect to buyers for sustainable commodities (product off-takers) to increase earnings. 
 
2. Support in economic improvement 
a. Honorarium for teaching staff, village officials, and field officer 
b. Providing scholarships 
c. Community empowerment 
d. Local employment absorption 
 
3. Development/provision of infrastructure facilities 
a. Maintenance of the village passageway  
b. Fuel assistance  
c. Construction of offices, village roads, etc 
 
4. Socio-cultural 
a. Commemoration of religious holidays package 
b. Sports and youth coaching 
c. General holidays package 
 
5. Participatory  conservation  of  forest resources  and  the  environment activities – environmental, 
agriculture, and forestry plants education seminar/socialization.

There  are  various  stakeholders  associated  with  this  project  and  other  interested  parties.  The  key 
project documents, which are the PD and monitoring reports will be available to all parties on the VCS 
project database. In addition, the Project Proponents will provide different levels of access to different 
parties  at  their  discretion  and  depending  on a  need-to-know basis  subject  to  request  submitted  to 
them. A list of identified stakeholder groups in below in Table 29. 
 
The  Project  Proponents  continue  to  engage  various  stakeholders  by  providing  them  with  relevant 
project  information  tailored  to  their  needs  and  roles.  Project  document  summaries  and  monitoring 
reports could be accessible to the stakeholders in the Verra project database when published. For the 
local community, summaries will be made available in hardcopy at the village office in local language 
(i.e.,  Bahasa  Indonesia),  and  project  updates  will  be  communicated  through  scheduling  perioding 
meetings when necessary.

2.3.4 Dissemination of Summary Project Documents (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, G3.1) 
For  early  and  transparent  information-sharing,  the  Project  Proponents  conducted  Free,  Prior,  and 
Informed  Consent  (FPIC)  processes, complemented  by  informal  and  formal  engagements  to 
communicate details about the proposed VCS project, its targeted impacts, and associated benefits 
to the communities involved. 
 
Since no communities reside within the immediate project area, the project is not expected to result in 
any  negative  impacts  on  local  populations.  However,  it  presents  a  significant  opportunity  for 
communities  in  the  surrounding  villages,  particularly  those  previously  involved  in  illegal  forestry 
activities, to transition into sustainable and legally supported livelihoods through equal employment 
opportunities and collaborative partnerships. 
 
As part of this transition, the project is working actively and collaboratively with local communities to 
develop sustainable fisheries and other community-based commodities, ensuring long-term economic 
benefits while reducing pressure on forest resources. By integrating community-led fisheries initiatives, 
ecotourism  enterprises,  and  value-added  production  of  non-timber  forest  products  (NTFPs),  the 
project fosters economic resilience and conservation stewardship within these communities. 
 
To  sustain  engagement,  the  project  will  ensure  that  information  is  continuously  accessible  to 
community  members  who  participated  in  the  FPIC  process  and  remain  involved  in  project 
implementation. The community partnership program, initiated by the Project Proponents, will further 
support  ongoing  dissemination  efforts  and  provide  local  stakeholders  with  critical  updates  and 
resources. 
 
Summary project documents will be available in both English and Indonesian, accessible in: 
• Softcopy – Published on the Verra database website and the Project Proponent’s website 
• Hardcopy – Available at village offices for community members to review 
• Community meetings – Shared through regular village discussions and workshops 
Additionally, the Project Proponents will continuously assess the dissemination process to ensure that 
all  stakeholders  receive  and  understand  the  project  details,  supporting  full  transparency  and 
meaningful participation. 
2.3.5 Informational Meetings with Stakeholders (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, G3.1) 
The Project  Proponents’ team  has been engaging with  the  communities  in  the  three  villages in  the 
project zone following the FPIC principles. The initial meeting took place on 10 March 2022 to discuss 
the management changes at PT. Kandelia Alam following the company's takeover. Attendees included 
NGOs, village government representatives, and the Village Forest Management Institution (or, LPHD), 
where  the  project  team  outlined  upcoming  activities  such  as  the  development  of the  VCS  PD and 
potential  sustainable  livelihood  initiatives.  A  subsequent  meeting  was  held  on  30  January  2023  to 
present  the  2023  work  plan,  including  the  delineation  of  protected  areas  and  work  boundaries, 
attended  by  43  participants  from  the  project  team  and  stakeholders.  Rapid  rural  appraisals  were 
conducted on 19 and 28 October 2023 to assess community resources, including natural assets, public 
facilities, community lands, and local issues. 
 
The Project   Proponents’ team   identified   that   certain   processes   must   be   completed   before 
implementing  social  programs  involving  the  community  to  inform  stakeholders  about  the  project's 
benefits. A public consultation for this project was conducted on 6 March 2024, with objectives to guide 
the  project's  consultation  activities,  enable  two-way  information  flow  between  the  project  team  and 
stakeholders,  and  address  and  document  stakeholder  feedback  and  concerns  regarding  project 
operations.

2.3.7 Community Costs, Risks, and Benefits (CCB, G3.2) 
The  communities  around  the  project  area  reside  in  the  project  zone. The  FPIC  processes  have 
identified their needs and also the representatives. Though no additional potential costs and risks are 
identified, potential  benefits  to  communities  are  identified,  any  changes  will  be  informed  via  the 
identified representatives as and when detected during the project implementation and operation. 
 
Through the implementation of the Sustainable Commodities programs such as sustainable fisheries 
(mudcrab & croaker fish), research-based ecotourism, NTFP developments communities will generate 
their  own  business  model  for  each commodity that  will  ensure  increase  of  income  beyond  the 
implementation of the carbon project/interventions since both will be implemented in parallel and not 
dependent on one another. 
 
2.3.8 Information to Stakeholders on Validation and Verification Process (VCS, 3.18.6, 3.19; 
CCB, G3.3) 
The  communities  within  the  project  zone  have  been  briefed  about  the  project,  including  upcoming 
validation and verification events where selected community members may be invited to speak with 
the VVB as representatives. Generally, the community will be notified about their potential participation 
in  these  events  several  weeks  in  advance,  typically  three  weeks  prior,  to  ensure  ample  time  for 
scheduling and mobilization of the relevant representatives. 
 
Logistical  arrangements,  including  agenda  preparation,  travel  routes  considering  distances  and 
transportation between villages and the project site, and venue selection, will be planned and finalized 
ahead of each validation or verification. 
 
2.3.9 Site Visit Information and Opportunities to Communicate with Auditor (VCS, 3.18.6; 
CCB, G3.3) 
Upon  the  verification  and  validation  by  VVB,  the Project  Proponents’ will  announce  informally  and 
formally the event to the community and stakeholder chosen by the auditor along with how they can 
communicate with the auditor, such as through face-to-face meeting or email to the auditor or call with 
the  auditor to  establish a documentary trail  of  events  leading  to  and  completion  of  the  consultation 
processes during any validation and verification event. Necessary sign-offs on all discussions will be 
recorded for future reference in the project (also refer documentation provisions provided in section 
2.3.5).
