3.4 Optional Criterion: Climate Change Adaptation Benefits  
3.4.1 Regional Climate Change Scenarios (CCB, GL1.1) 
As part of the Indonesia Enhanced Nationally Determined Contribution (ENDC), adaptation is part of 
the Indonesian government's strategic plan to fight climate change. Within one of ENDC's strategies, 
Indonesia will  promote  climate  resilience  in  food,  water,  and  energy:  recognizing  the  importance  of 
meeting  the  food,  water  and  energy  needs  of  a  growing  young  population,  Indonesia  will  improve 
natural resource management to increase climate resilience by protecting and restoring key terrestrial 
resources, coastal and marine ecosystems. 
The  Government  of  Indonesia  has  made  significant  efforts  in  developing  and  implementing  the 
National  Action  Plan  for  Climate  Change  Adaptation  which  provides  a  framework  for  adaptation 
initiatives  that  have  been  mainstreamed  into  the  National  Development  Plan.  In  order  to  fulfill  this 
commitment,  the  Indonesian  government  has  made  Climate  Resilient  Development  one  of  the  6th 
(sixth) National Priorities (PN) in the National Medium Term Development Plan (RPJMN) 2020 – 2024, 
namely Building the Living Environment, Increasing Disaster Resilience and Climate Change has been 
determined through President Regulation (Perpres) No. 18 of 2020. 
The  targets  and  indicators  set  in  the Climate Resilient  Development  action  are  the  percentage  (%) 
reduction in GDP decline in four priority sectors, namely the Marine and Coastal Sector, Water Sector, 
Agricultural  Sector  and  Health  Sector.  It  is  hoped  that various  policy  interventions  and  climate 
resilience activities carried out in the four priority sectors can reduce potential economic losses due to 
climate change. West Kalimantan province is one of the priority areas for climate resilient development 
actions. Kubu Raya Regency, which is part of West Kalimantan province, is one of the top priorities in 
Climate  Resilient  Development  actions,  especially  in  the  marine  and  coastal  sectors  and  the 
agricultural sector. 
The government has developed a national climate vulnerability index data information system called 
SIDIK  (Vulnerability  Index  Data  Information  System)  and  can  be  accessed  publicly.  Likewise,  the 
Indonesian  government  has  issued  Ministerial  Regulation  No  P.7/2018  concerning  Guidelines  for 
Assessment  of  Vulnerabilities,  Risks  and  Impacts  of  Climate  Change  and  P.33/2016  concerning 
Guidelines for Developing Adaptation Actions, which allows central and regional governments to carry 
out assessments, formulate and implement actions. adaptation in their respective regions. 
3.4.2 Climate Change Impacts (CCB, GL1.2) 
Significant  number  of  the  community  is  living  as  farmer,  breeder,  fisherman  that  susceptible  to  the 
climate  change  due  to  the  impact  of  weather  uncertainty  that  affect  wave  occurrence,  harvest 
uncertainty, pest and diseases, and rainwater to supply the farmer’s land is decreasing. The following 
is the type of livelihood that is affected by climate change: 
 
Table 87: Types of job and breakdown of each village 
No. Type of Job Kubu Village Batu Ampar village 
Number Unit Number Unit 
1. Farmer 1,061 People 1,237 People 
2. Farm Labor 67 People 311 People 
3. Breeder  32 People 92 People 
4. Fisherman  257 People 356 People 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
175 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.3 
 
Based on comparison with total population, there are 24.39% of the community in Kubu village and 
21.07% of the community in Batu Ampar that is potentially affected by climate change as mentioned 
above. 
 
Indonesia  has  website  showing  Vulnerability  Index  Data  Information  System  (SIDIK)  for  each  sub 
district. The vulnerability index for Kubu Raya Regency is categorized as 3. The vulnerability index is 
affected by the Adaptive Vulnerability Index (IKA) and Exposure and Sensitivity Index (IKS) with the 
following elements. 
 
Table 88: Elements of IKA and IKS 
No IKA IKS 
1 Road Community density 
2 Health Facility Source of fuel 
3 Education Facility Defecate facility 
4 Electricity Facility Type of trash bin 
5 Community Institution Main livelihood 
6 Communication Source of freshwater 
7 Small and Micro Industry Poverty level 
8 Economy Infrastructure Topography of most village area 
9 Credit facility received by 
community 
 
10 Financial institution  
11 Environment conservation activity  
12 Health insurance  
 
Climate change impact on forest ecosystem includes the changes of flowering time that will result with 
the  changes  of  fruit  season.  The  other  impact  is  the  raining  and  dry  season  that  becoming 
unpredictable  as  temporary  creek,  temporary  water  pond,  and  Salt  Lake  will  have  uncertainty  for 
replenishment.  These  together  could  affect  wildlife  behavior,  life  cycle,  and  competitiveness.  This 
could  have  significant  impact  to  the  endangered  species  in  the  project  area  such  as  Proboscis 
Monkeys  (Nasalis  larvatus),  Long  Tailed  Macaque  (Macaca  fascicularis),  xxx  macaque  (Macaca 
nemstrina), and Irrawady Dolphins (Orcaella brevirostris). 
 
The climate change will also cause decrease in rainfall, increased maximum temperatures, and shifts 
in climate pattern thus will cause prolong drought that will impact the rehabilitation and restoration of 
open area and shrubs in project area. This condition is anticipated through efforts to increase forest 
cover by considering adaptive species and water providers, as described in Chapter 5. 
3.4.3 Measures Needed and Designed for Adaptation (CCB, GL1.3) 
No adaptation needs for the communities from probable impacts of climate change are applicable to 
this project. However, the project area could in future be impacted by rising water levels and erosion, 
and the re-planting activities included in the project are expected to mitigate such impacts.

176 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.3 
4 COMMUNITY 
4.1 Without-Project Community Scenario  
4.1.1 Descriptions of Communities at Project Start (CCB, CM1.1) 
Continuing  from  information  included in section 2.1.15 of  this  PD  on the  profile  of  the communities 
residing  in  the  three  villages  in  the  project  zone  but  outside  the  project  area  such  as  on human 
resources,  public  facility,  natural  resources,  type  of  livelihood,  history, further  information included 
below. 
 
The  communities  in  Kubu  and  Batu  Ampar villages are predominantly  Malay,  with  a diverse mix  of 
Dayak, Javanese, and Chinese ethnic population. The livelihoods for the majority are heavily rely on 
exploitation  of  local natural  resources,  primarily  through  farming  and  fishing,  which  are  the  main 
occupations  in  both  villages. The  average  monthly  income  ranges  around  IDR  1,000,000  to  IDR 
3,000,000. 
 
In Kubu village, the majority religion is Islam, and the rest are Christians, Catholics, and others, and 
perform  religious  ceremonies  as  per individual  faiths.  Furthermore,  the  local  customary culture has 
been preserved and observed; customary ceremonies are continuously carried out by the community 
for generations to ensure that the culture is not lost. 
 
Batu Ampar village, the majority religion is Islam, and the rest are Christians, Catholics, and Buddhist. 
The local  communities  continue  to perform ancestral traditional  rituals, such  as, Robo-robo,  land 
clearing ceremonies, village feeding and others. In addition, customary law is still applicable for Batu 
Ampar  village.  The  average monthly  income  for  local workers is between  IDR1,500,000  and 
IDR2,700,000. 
 
However, some community members are engaged in illegal logging activities within the project area. 
Additionally, many fishermen continue to use harmful methods such as poison, bombs, and electricity 
to catch fish in mangrove areas. These unsustainable practices not only jeopardize the sustainability 
of fish stocks but also cause substantial damage to marine habitats. 
 
According  to  the Project  Proponent's  assessments  and  community  interviews,  mangrove  charcoal 
production though not a dominant livelihood activity among local residents, continues as a traditional 
practice  passed  down  through  generations.  In  Kubu village,  the  number  of  charcoal  stoves  has 
decreased over  time with  increased  awareness  on  environmental  and  health  impacts,  with  an 
estimated  5-10  furnaces  still  in  use. In Batu Ampar  village, approximately  300-400  stoves are 
estimated to be in use. 
 
Most mangrove charcoal workers engage in this activity on part-time basis, particularly during periods 
of low fish catches, thereby contributing to the pressure on the eco-system in areas within the project 
boundaries. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
177 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.3 
 
The  increasing  demand  for  Tirus  fish  (Johnius  sp),  prized  for  their  respiratory  bubbles  used  in 
cosmetics  and  medicinal  products,  has  driven  a  surge  in  nibung  (Onchosperma  tigillarium)  wood 
cultivation for making fish cages. This heightened demand for nibung wood places additional strain on 
mangrove forests, posing a further threat to this eco-system.

The FPIC report as well as previous interactions, communications and consultations with communities 
and stakeholders in the project zone, as discussed in sub-sections of section 2 in this PD, provides 
information on the two-way communications undertaken since the inception of this project, and for the 
benefit of all relevant stakeholders.

The anticipated changes in the well-being and other characteristics of communities and community

the Environmental Impact Assessment (called ‘AMDAL’ in Indonesian regulations) and the business

These obligations necessitate the creation of various benefits for the surrounding communities (those

a. creating equal employment opportunities for local residents, 
b. implementing alternative poverty reduction initiatives, and

4.2.2 Negative Community Impact Mitigation (VCS, 3.19; CCB, CM2.2) 
No negative impact on community is identified. It is anticipated that the implementation of the project 
will not have any negative impacts (pollution and safety issues) on individuals and members of the 
community groups. However, those individuals who are engaged in illegal logging will not have any 
income  from  such  illegal  activities.  The Project  Proponents plan  to create alternative  livelihood for 
some  of  those individuals  such  as  by  providing equal  employment  opportunity  and  collaborative 
partnerships in the project as rangers (POKMASWAS).

4.2.4 High Conservation Values Protected (CCB, CM2.4) 
The Project Proponent has conducted HCV assessment 2014 and has identified HCV attributes in the 
project area which are HCV 4 (for 4.1 and 4.3) and HCV 6. The HCV area has been marked in the 
field and has been excluded from the past harvesting operation and will continue to be protected. The 
Project  Proponent stopped  their  harvesting  operation  since  2022  and is  committed  to  continuing 
protecting the identified  areas  with HCV  attributes (also  refer  section  4.1.3  of  this  PD) through  the 
entire duration of the project and beyond.

The Project Proponents intend to communicate information to interested stakeholders regarding the 
monitoring plan included in this PD. Any stakeholder if needing additional information such as results 
of performance monitoring could check the periodic monitoring reports over the project’s crediting 
period. The  information,  except  those  that  are  commercially  sensitive, will  be  available  publicly  at

4.5 Optional Criterion: Exceptional Community Benefits  
4.5.1 Exceptional Community Criteria (CCB, GL2.1) 
Indonesia
34
 has   been a   high   Human   Development Index (“HDI”) country at   the time   of 
inception/conceptualisation of this project in 2021 with HDI of 0.705. However, as per national statistics 
at least 50% of the households within the communities are below the national poverty line. 
 
The project zone is in a low human development region that needs sustainable development for all 
spheres of life including education and healthcare. The communities follow local cultural practices and 
self-survival  practices.  No  other  exceptional  community  criteria  are  identified  by  the Project 
Proponents in the project zone. 
4.5.2 Short-term and Long-term Community Benefits (CCB, GL2.2) 
There are five aspects to the impact of a project on people's lives: 1) economic; 2) natural access; 3) 
human capital; 4) physical; and 5) social. The impacts of the project on the community can be divided 
into two categories: short-term and long-term. Short-term impact is defined as impacts within one to 
three years, while long-term impact is defined as impacts ten years after the project's completion. It is 
anticipated that the project will create positive impacts for the surrounding communities living in the 
project  zone  by establishing  community-led  business  model  generation  to  provide sustainable 
livelihoods and by preventing activities that will gradually lead to climate change and negative impacts 
of climate change. Also, please refer to the ‘Theory of Change’ included in section 2.1.17. 
 
4.5.3 Community Participation Risks (CCB, GL2.3) 
Since none of the communities in the project zone reside within the project area and does not have 
any  ownership  and  stake  in  any  legal  activities  and  local  customary  activities  within  the  project 
boundary, then no local community is relevant to be will not be involved in any primary decision-making 
processes for this VCS project. As such there are no community participation risks to the VCS project. 
 
However, their input may be considered during the planning and operations of the project activities to 
improve their socio-economic conditions and create sustainable development outcomes. 
 
In addition, the community will participate in the technical project implementation activities, exposing 
them to potential risk including work incidents, and suitable training programs have been planned for 
such individuals. To mitigate  these  type  of  risks,  necessary trainings will  be  provided. According  to 
their Business Work Plan for Forest Product Utilization (RKUPH), the Project Proponents will assist 
with improving the quality of health facilities and infrastructure to mitigate any other operational risks. 
4.5.4 Marginalized and/or Vulnerable Community Groups (CCB, GL2.4) 
Not applicable for this project area and surrounding project zone. 
 
 
 
34
 Human Development Index | Human Development Reports (undp.org) 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template

CCB v3.0, VCS v4.3 
4.5.5 Net Impacts on Women (CCB, GL2.5) 
This project  activity  is  expected  to enhance  the  empowerment  and  well-being  of  women in  the 
communities residing in the villages in the project zone. This project will result in the following positive 
outcomes for women: 
 
a. Health benefits from exposure to smoke by gradually reducing local charcoal making activities 
and use of firewood for sugar making industry. 
 
b. Education related opportunities with financial/scholarship support. 
4.5.6 Benefit Sharing Mechanisms (CCB, GL2.6) 
Not applicable. 
4.5.7 Benefits, Costs, and Risks Communication (VCS, 3.18; CCB, GL2.7) 
Based  on  the  Business  Work  Plan  for  Forest  Product  Utilization  (RKUPH),  the communities  (i.e., 
residents  of  the  three  villages  in  the  project  zone  outside  the  project  area) will  be  informed  at  the 
beginning  of  the  project  and  periodically  during  operation  of  the  projects  to  create  awareness  on 
potential benefits and risks if any of the communities continue to work illegally inside the project area. 
The Project Proponents have also drafted a potential solution to mitigate the risks when they occur. 
The FPIC that was  conducted earlier  has  also  assisted  with  improving  communication  with  the 
communities  on  all  relevant  topics.  As  a  part  of  this  FPIC,  the Project  Proponents tried  to  map  the 
community's  existence,  location of  residence,  and  areas within  the project area where  some 
community members illegally harvested timber. The Project Proponents also tried to understand the 
land  inventory  within  the regulatory concession boundary  that  defines  the  project area,  aiming  to 
identify if there is any overlapping claims by any parties.

Refer to the subsections in section 2.4 of this PD. 
4.5.9 Smallholders/Community Members Capacity Development (CCB, GL2.9) 
This is planned to be implemented as required in the business license and project activity approval 
licenses (RKU) by the Indonesian government agencies.

5 BIODIVERSITY 
5.1 Without-Project Biodiversity Scenario  
5.1.1 Existing Conditions (VCS, 3.19; CCB, B1.1) 
Flora/Vegetation 
The project area is situated within an estuarine landscape predominantly dominated by mangroves, 
organized  into  various  zones  and  formations  that  naturally  sustain  a  cohesive  coastal  swamp 
ecosystem. Mangrove ecosystems typically encompass four distinct zones: seaward, mid, landward, 
and terrestrial. In the project area, while there are no seaward zones directly facing the sea, the other 
three zones are present. 
 
Vegetation  in  the  project  area  predominantly  consists  of  mid  mangrove  forests,  the  most  prevalent 
type in Indonesia. Mid mangroves thrive in transitional ecological zones, such as estuaries, positioned 
between  open  seafronts  and  inland  areas  occasionally  inundated  by  seawater  during  high  tides. 
Locations  further  inland  and  away  from  the  waterbodies/creeks  that  are  occasionally  and  only 
inundated during high tides are vegetated with rear mangroves stretching between 100m to 2,000m 
in  width.  Beyond  the  terrestrial  mangroves  lie  dryland  forests  or  freshwater  swamp  forests,  where 
certain tree species exceed the typical stature of terrestrial mangroves in terms of height and spread.  
 
Mid mangroves, sheltered from strong wave forces of the open sea, predominantly occupy flat muddy 
terrains along tidal inlets and rivers in the project area. Despite periodic inundation by seawater during 
high tides, the soil remains partially saturated even during low tides. Apart from rainwater, freshwater 
influx into central mangroves is minimal. Key species in the mid zone include Rhizophora apiculata, 
Bruguiera gymnorrhiza, and Xylocarpus granatum, with the rare Kandelia candel found primarily at the 
mouths of small rivers within the broader mid zone. 
 
The  eastern  and  northern  regions  of  the  project  area  are  predominantly  covered  by  landward 
mangroves,  extending  upstream  along  rivers  like  Rada,  Pa’awal,  and  Keluang.  The  Sapar  sub-
watershed features extensive terrestrial mangroves ranging from Nipah swamps to freshwater forests 
in  the  northernmost  regions.  However,  landward mangroves  along  the Keluang  river do  not  extend 
beyond  its  confluence  with  the  Kelabau  river.  These  mangroves  occupy  a  significant  portion  of  the 
Kalabau sub-watershed. 
 
The  transition  from  mid  mangroves  to  Nipah  swamps  and  subsequently  to  landward mangroves  is 
evident along the Keluang river, where Nipah swamps dominate the middle reaches, while the Safar 
river downstream areas are largely Nipah-dominated. 
 
Scattered clumps and solitary palm trees of Nibung (Onchosperma tigillarium) are found in terrestrial 
mangrove  zones,  often  indicating  the  transition  from  mangrove  areas  to  freshwater  swamp 
ecosystems. These formations typically grow densely or mixed with other vegetation types. 
 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
190 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.3 
Freshwater-fed  forests  are  completely  unreachable  by  tides  and  the  influence  of  sea  water.  In  this 
area  there  are  dry  land  forests  or  freshwater  swamp  forests  behind  mangrove  forests  or  Nibung 
formation. In the project area, freshwater swamps can often be identified by open areas dominated by 
bushes dan grass (Poaceae and Cyperaceae), and Rasau (Pandanus sp). 
  
The  number  of  flora  species  found  in  the  project  area  is  187  species.  Based  on  IUCN  Red  List 
categories, there is 2 species categorized as Endagered Species (EN) namely Dipterocarpus retusus 
and  Syzygium  zeylanicum,  and  4  species  categorized  as  Vulnerable Species  (VU)  namely  Dyera 
polyphylla, Shorea balangeran, Crudia scortechinii, and Knema krusemaniana. 
 
Fauna 
The  total  number  of  species  of  wildlife  on  Mammals,  Birds,  Reptiles,  Amphibians,  Fishes,  Insects, 
Crustaceans, and Clams are for amount 243 species, which consists of 24 species of Mammal, 84 
species of Bird, 22 species of Reptile, 7 species of Amphibian, 86 species of Fishes, 13 species of 
Insect, 6 species of Crustacean, and 1 species of Clam. Based on IUCN Red List categories, there 
are 6 species categorized as Endangered Species (EN) namely Macaca fascicularis, xxx macaque 
(Macaca nemstrina), Nasalis larvatus, Orcaella brevirostris, Heosemys spinosa, Lamiopsis tephrodes, 
and Himantura undulata. There are 9 species categorized as Vulnerable Species (VU) namely Cervus 
unicolor,   Sus   barbatus,   Trachypithecus   cristatus,   Helarctos   malayanus,   Buceros   rhinoceros, 
Leptoptilos javanicus, Treron fulvicollis, Brevitrygon heterura, Chiloscyllium indicum. 
 
Due  to  the  threats  from  illegal  logging  of  mangrove  and  nibung  trees,  the  forest  structure  and 
composition that eventually affect the population and long-term survival of Proboscis Monkey (Nasalis 
larvatus), Long Tailed Macaque (Macaca fascicularis) and xxx macaque (Macaca nemstrina). While 
the uses of  bomb,  poison,  and  electricity  for  fishing  by  the  community  will  threaten  the  habitat  of 
Irrawady Dolphins (Orcaella brevirostris) as well as their population and long-term survival. Thus, the 
project create program to also cover the needs to conserve this biodiversity by conducting patrol, the 
use of satellite imagery and drone to detect any disturbance of the forest cover, providing alternative 
sustainable livelihood for the community to further reduce the threats.

5.1.2 High Conservation Values (CCB, B1.2) 
Typically,  any  High  Conservation  Values  (HCV)  area  may  have  conditions involving one  or more 
biological, ecological, social, or cultural values that are deemed nationally, regionally, or globally to be 
of exceptional or critical significance. According to the Project's High Conservation Value Management 
and Monitoring Plan prepared in 2015, five out of six defined HCVs are available in their project area. 
To ensure that no HCVs are endangered, it is essential for the Project Proponent to carefully evaluate 
the project activities that are relevant to implementation of this eco-system conservation project and 
conditions in its license. The following HCVs are located within the project boundary.

The project area is bordered with protected areas, namely the Simpang 
Cabe protected forest and the P. Perling protected forest, which are 
located to the south of the Project Proponent area, and areas directly 
adjacent  to  the  Simpang  Cabe protected  forest and  the  P.  Perling

Radak hill, and germplasm conservation area/ in situ conservation area. 
Because  the  Simpang  Cabe protected  forest and  Perling  Island 
protected  forest are  interconnected,  the  existence  of the  locally 
protected areas provides an ecological function to support biodiversity. 
Based on these findings, the Project Proponents have identified an area 
containing HCV1.1 which span over 743.19 hectares.

endangered status  according  to  IUCN.  Proboscis  Monkey  (Nasalis 
larvatus)  and  Irrawady  Dolphins  (Orcaella  brevirostris)  listed  as 
protected  species  under  the  Ministry  of  Environment  and  Forestry 
Decree  No.  106/2018.  To  date,  the  ongoing  population  decline  has 
prompted further consideration for designation as an HCV1.2 species. 
The Keluang river border and the Kelabau river border are among the 
locations that become HCV 1.2 which span an area of 255.06 hectares.

threatened species is not supported by the availability of habitat, it will 
be difficult for the species to survive in the future. Observational findings 
indicate that several areas in the Project Proponent region continue to 
serve the useful function of habitat for the species named above. 
 
Some of these species include: 
1. Dolphins (Orcaella brevirostris) that occasionally visit the waters of 
the Sepada river, approximately two kilometers from the river's mouth.

recently cleared areas for the ‘Permanent Measuring Plots (“PUP”)’. 
 
3. Cyornis rufigastra (Mangrove Fly) and Mangrove Honey bird have 
specific habitat requirements  within  mangrove eco-system,  making 
adaptation to non-mangrove habitats challenging. 
 
In terms of existing wildlife, the above findings and analysis indicate that 
within the project area, protected and threatened species classified as 
vulnerable and endangered were identified. 
 
The forests within and in the vicinity of project area offer right habitat 
conditions for  the  species identified  above.  Therefore,  the Project 
Proponents have established  a  protected  area  dedicated  to  the 
conservation of native flora and fauna.

Cabe, Bukit Radak, germplasm conservation area/ in situ conservation

Sepada Left river, and covers an area of 4,057 hectares.

5.1.3 Without-project Scenario: Biodiversity (CCB, B1.3) 
The  communities  surrounding  the  project  area in  the  project  zone  have  been converting  the 
neighboring mangrove forest into fishponds, which explains why these ponds are so common in the 
mangrove  forest. These  activities  threaten  the mangrove eco-system  with  deforestation  and 
degradation. 
 
Furthermore, some of the community members in the villages located in the project zone are involved 
in charcoal/briquettes business that involves illegal extraction of timber from forests in the project area 
(in  this  case  mangrove  wood). These  activities  are  also adversely  affecting  the eco-system  and 
biodiversity in the and around the project area from continuous deforestation and forest degradation. 
 
The  without-project  scenario mentioned  above will continuously worsen due  to both illegal forest 
land/area grabbing activities and illegal logging in the project area.

5.2.1 Expected Biodiversity Changes (VCS, 3.19; CCB, B2.1)

5.2.2 Mitigation Measures (VCS, 3.19; CCB, B2.3) 
According  to  the project’s  Environmental  and  Social  Impact  Assessment  (AMDAL  by  Indonesian 
acronym) for previously undertaking commercial logging activities, it was assessed that the diversity 
of birds, mammals, and other species will change or decrease, particularly in logged-over areas as a 
secondary effect of changes in habitat conditions caused by logging. The destruction of habitat caused 
by logging is detrimental to the habitat of wildlife in general. Species that cannot tolerate changes in 
habitat conditions would have disappeared over time in the project area and in the surroundings, while 
those  that  can  adapt would seek  out  new  habitats. Such  changes  will  also  occur  illegal  logging 
continues in the project area. 
 
As part of the Environmental Management and Monitoring Activities section of the Project Proponent's 
business  plan  for 2022-2031, a  matrix  containing  Environmental  Management  and  Monitoring  Plan 
Matrix that monitors the change in diversity of fauna due to logging and forest clearing is available. 
These activities in the matrix have several plans, including: (1) Reducing the impact of infrastructure 
construction (PWH by Indonesian acronym) and logging activities on the decline of fauna diversity in 
the study area; (2) Efforts to restore ecosystems resulting from human destructive activities; and (3) 
Optimizing  the  function  of  the  greenbelt,  revegetating  unproductive  land,  and  preserving  wildlife 
habitats in natural forest ecosystems used as project activity areas are goals. 
 
While  the  logging  activities  will  be  discontinued,  and  illegal  logging  activities  will  be  prevented  by 
implementation  of  this  eco-system  conservation  project,  the  Environmental  Management  and 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
197 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.3 
Monitoring  Plan  as  well  as  (2) and  (3)  above  will  be  continued  to  ensure  environmental  and  social 
impacts are mitigated. 
5.2.3 Net Positive Biodiversity Impacts (CCB, B2.2, GL1.4) 
The net positive biodiversity impacts are summarised in section 2.1.17 and in Appendix 2 of this PD 
through describing the ‘Theory of Change’. 
5.2.4 High Conservation Values Protected (CCB, B2.4) 
As explained in section 4.2.4 of this PD, the planned project activities will have no negative impacts 
on  the HCV areas  in  the  project area  and  project zone. Under the  new  business plan,  which  is  for 
implementing the project, the primary objective of the project has always been to utilize the forest for 
its non-timber products and environmental services. Under this new business plan, logging will cease, 
and the emphasis will shift to the restoration of the native mangrove and swamp forests. In the coming 
years, it is anticipated that over 15.6 million seedlings will be planted across the areas designated for 
‘restoration of wetland ecosystems (“RWE”)’ with re-planting activities. In addition, several activities 
will  be  conducted  as  part  of  forest  security  and  protection,  including  the  prevention  of  forest 
encroachment, preservation of soil and water, prevention of illegal logging, and installation of warning 
signs. These activities will safeguard the HCVs of the project area.

No fertilizers, chemical pesticides, biological control agents, or other inputs will be used for this project, 
so this section is not applicable. 
5.2.9 Waste Products (VCS, 3.19; CCB, B2.9) 
It  is  expected  that  presence  of project  implementation  and  operations  teams,  the  creation  and 
execution  of  patrolling  operation  and  infrastructure  such  as  base/control  camp  for  the  project  will 
generate general domestic wastes as well as operational hardware-based rejects. 
 
The Project  Proponents have  a  waste  management  plan  for  collection  and  safe  disposal  of  such 
wastes. However, the overall annual quantity is not expected to be significant. 
5.3 Offsite Biodiversity Impacts  
5.3.1 Negative Offsite Biodiversity Impacts (CCB, B3.1) and Mitigation Measures (CCB, B3.2) 
No potential negative impacts on biodiversity outside of the project zone resulting from project activities 
is expected. 
5.3.2 Net Offsite Biodiversity Benefits (VCS, 3.19; CCB, B3.3) 
The project is designed for delivering net positive biodiversity impacts as such needs were identified 
in previous studies conducted by different entities/agencies in the project area. The project activities 
are designed to deliver those positive benefits and hence no offsite biodiversity impacts are expected. 
These benefits include, for example, creating conditions that allow the animals (mammals and birds) 
move freely from outside the project area to inside, and vice versa, ensuring safe habitat for growth. 
This in turn provides secondary benefits in several ways, for example in spreading seeds, controlling 
insects, and assist in the natural regeneration of degraded land. Reports from previous biodiversity 
studies can be shared with the VVB during the project validation. 
5.4 Biodiversity Impact Monitoring  
5.4.1 Biodiversity Monitoring Plan (CCB, B4.1, B4.2, GL1.4, GL3.4) 
A monitoring plan for biodiversity will be utilised to cover the entire ecosystem in the project area. The 
monitoring plan will also refer to the HCV document, since the HCV area has been identified in the 
project area. This monitoring plan will include the following: 
 
• Monitoring activities: 
o flora and fauna monitoring at the plot level and in situ conservation area: this activity will 
monitor the existence of the flora and fauna in the project area through the transect permanent 
sample   plot   and in situ conservation   area that is   being determined   during   project 
implementation;  those flora  that  will  be identified  and  monitoring  include  species  of  Woody 
plant  and Herbaceous  plant,  while  those  fauna  that  will  be  identified  and monitored include 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
199 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.3 
species of mammal, bird, reptile, insect, amphibia, fish, crustacea, and Mollusca; this activity 
will be conducted at least every year, but also conditional depending on the behaviour of the 
key animals observed; the flora and fauna monitoring will be conducted for land and waters 
habitat  and  designed  to  be  able  to  monitor  the  population  of  Proboscis  Monkeys  (Nasalis 
larvatus),  Long  Tailed  Macaque  (Macaca  fascicularis), and  Irrawady  Dolphins  (Orcaella 
brevirostris) that classified as endangered species; 
o land cover change monitoring: this activity will monitor the quality of the natural habitat area 
for key species and rare ecosystems found in the project area; this activity will be conducted 
at least every year following the climate monitoring; 
o camera trap  installation:  this  activity  will  monitor  key  species  found  in  the  project  area; 
camera traps  will  be  installed  in  the  key  species  habitat  area;  the recorded  photos  will  be 
collected periodically depending on the capacity of the equipment and other resources; 
o monitoring, prevention and controlling to illegal logging, area encroachment, introduced and/or 
invasive species, forest and land fires carried out in collaboration with local security units; 
o activities to protect threatened (VU & EN according to IUCN Red List) plant and animal species 
by installing and maintaining prohibition board of protected animals and plants hunting, and 
do enrichment planting for threatened plant species in HCV areas; and 
o create awareness for employees and the local communities about protected plant and animal 
species in and around the project area. 
 
• Biodiversity variables to be monitored is explained in the procedure document. 
 
• The biodiversity monitoring team refers to section 2.4.2 and 2.4.3. 
 
 
Biodiversity periodic monitoring carried out by PT. Kandelia Alam in collaborations with universities, academia, and 
multi-stekholder. 
5.4.2 Biodiversity Monitoring Plan Dissemination (CCB, B4.3) 
The  summaries  of  monitoring  plan  and  the  result  of  monitoring will  be  uploaded  to the Project 
Proponent’s website.  The Project  Proponent will  present  the  monitoring  plan  and  the  result  of  the 
monitoring to the community in during meetings in the villages. Hardcopy communication on the results 
of monitoring will be made available in the village office for easier access by any community members 
in Indonesian local language version. 
 
Multi stakeholders such as the Natural Resource Conservation Agency (Balai Konservasi Sumberdaya 
Alam – BKSDA),  Faculty  of  Forestry  of  Tanjungpura  University,  Environment  and  Forestry  local 
Agency (Dinas LHK) of West Kalimantan province, and NGOs will be consulted separately or together 
when  necessary  to  obtain  inputs and  comments on  the project’s monitoring plan and the result of 
monitoring.

5.5 Optional Criterion: Exceptional Biodiversity Benefits  
5.5.1 High Biodiversity Conservation Priority Status (CCB, GL3.1) 
Based on the biodiversity assessment, 24 species of flora and 106 species of fauna in the project area 
(see Appendix 4 and Appendix 5) were identified. Based on the IUCN Red List, there are four species 
categorized  as  Endagered  Species  (EN)  consisting  of one flora,  and three fauna  species; the  flora 
species is Dipterocarpus retusus (Dipterocarpaceae), and the fauna species are Proboscis Monkeys 
(Nasalis  larvatus),  Long  Tailed  Macaque  (Macaca  fascicularis), xxx  macaque  (Macaca  nemstrina), 
and  Irrawady  Dolphin  (Orcaella  brevirostris). Further, seven species identified  are categorized  as 
Vulnerable  (VU)  consisting  of one flora and six fauna  species; the  flora is  Shorea  balangeran,  and 
fauna  species  are  Cervus  unicolor,  Sus  barbatus,  Trachypithecus  cristatus,  Buceros  rhinoceros, 
Leptoptilos javanicus, and Treron fulvicollis. 
5.5.2 Trigger Species Population Trends (CCB, GL3.2, GL3.3) 
 
Table 108: Trends in trigger species populations 
 
Trigger 
species 
Proboscis Monkey (Nasalis larvatus) classified as endangered by 
IUCN Red List 
Population 
trend at the 
start of

204 individuals with estimated population of 266-316
35
 
Without-
project 
scenario 
Bekantan/Proboscis monkey population (Nasalis larvatus) were identified 
from  direct  field  observations  in certain  location  within  the  project (at 
Lungai Kelabau, Apar Left and Apar Right) as well as the surrounding 
area in the project zone and was estimated to be around 204 individuals. 
 
At the site of the mangrove forest of Strait of Sekh there is no population 
or signs of these monkeys; this location has similar type of ecosystem to 
that generally seen in a proximal zone (i.e., near the sea) where usually 
the number of species of trees are limited to three to five species such as 
Rhizophora  apiculata,  Rhizophora  mucronata  and  Sonneratia  alba. 
Hence,  due  to  minimal food  types  (shoots,  leaves)  the  nutritional 
requirements of these monkeys are not met as a preferable habitat. Since 
the  quality  and  quantity  of  feed  can  affect  the  behavior  and  social 
organization  of  primates,  the  size  of  the  home  range  and  movement 
behavior of primates are also impacted including influences on growth 
and reproduction. Feed potential also influences the size of the proboscis 
monkey group and population. 
 
The local ecosystem  with  all  the  interaction  processes  and  inter-
relationships of its components that influence animal population growth 
based on availability or potential of food in its habitat. In addition, the 
 
35
 PT. Bina Ovivipari Semesta and PT. Kandelia Alam. 2015, Probosic Monkey Survey Report on PT. 
Bina Ovivipari Semesta and PT. Kandelia Alam. Not published. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
201 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.3 
presence,  position, and  condition  of resting/sleeping locations  on  the 
trees determine the carrying capacity of the habitat. In this regard, the 
mangrove forest in the proximal zone (close to the sea) is considered to 
be not preferred by the proboscis monkeys because in this zone the sea 
breeze is very strong thereby disturbing their resting needs. Availability of 
safe tree-top resting locations is also their main need, because according 
to  observations,  proboscis  monkeys  rest  more  after  actively  eating 
(42.3% of the time). During resting periods, ruminant animals process 
food in the digestive tract and at this time a lot of heat is released through 
the digestive tract. To maintain temperature balance, proboscis monkeys 
rest or sleep in a sitting position with their limbs pressed close to their 
body parts so that heat release by convection and evaporation can be 
reduced.  
 
Map showing  likely  distribution of  proboscis  monkey habitats in 
conservation areas in West Kalimantan province
36
 
 
Based on observations in the project area (Kelabau river, Apar and Apar 
Kanan) and the surrounding areas, the forest conditions could be claimed 
to  be relatively  good.  There  are  many  food  trees  available  such  as 
mangrove trees (Rhizopora apiculata), Dungun (Heritiera littoralis), Nyirih 
(Xylocarpus  granatum),  Pedada  (Sonneratia  sp),  and  Jejawi  (Ficus 
microcarpa), as well as the large distribution of tree complexes for resting 
(around 50  complexes)  or  more;  this  indicate that  the  population  of 
proboscis monkeys in this area is quite large, according to estimates it 
can reach 250 – 300 individuals. Of the number of individuals seen, some 
(20 groups or 150 individuals) were outside the project area, but judging 
from the presence of resting places on the trees, it is very likely that the 
daily movement (homerange) of these groups is also within the project 
area (on the Radak, Sepada, Kelabau, Apar and Apar Kanan rivers).

Currently  there  are  5,192 hectares of  degraded  area  that indicates 
reduction in the  habitat  for  the  proboscis  monkeys.  There  are  also 
changes in  habitat such as  reduced Nibung stands which  are  playing 
areas  for  these  primates.  Nibung  has  economic value for  the local 
community who illegally cut these trees. 
 
Under  the ‘Action  Plan of  Proboscis  monkey  corridor  Management’ in 
Kubu Landscape in 2021, the project proponents were identified as an 
important stakeholder in the programme to creatcontinues a proboscis 
monkey corridor together with other companies operating businesses in 
the Kubu  Landscape.  This  is  detail  plan  of  the  Proboscis  monkey’s 
conservation  strategy  and  action  plan  of  2013-2022  from  Ministry  of 
Forestry Decree No. P.56/2013. 
With-project 
scenario 
The  Project  Proponent  will  stop harvesting  operation,  restore  the 
degraded area, and develop alternative sustainable livelihood to reduce 
illegal logging by the community that existed in the pre-project scenario. 
It is expected that the habitat for proboscis monkey will be improved. 
Resources will be better allocated to monitor the habitat and population 
of proboscis monkey.

H. Bivalvia (Clams) 
1 
Polymesoda erosa Cyrenidae 
  20               
 
 
Source: High Conservation Values (2014) and Biodiversity Monitoring Report (2023) 
 
Information : 
Endemic 
B : Endemic to Borneo 
GS : Endemic to the Greater Sunda Islands (Borneo, Sumatra, 
Java & Bali) 
 
Migrant 
BR+M : Breeding residents with populations seasonally augmented 
by non-breeding visitors 
M : Northern temperate 'winter' migrants: on Borneo from 
November to April 
Mn : Migrants restricted mostly to northern Borneo 
Ms : Northern temperate 'summer' migrants: on Borneo mostly May 
to October 
Habitat 
A : Aerial 
C : Coastal 
F : Forest species (Closed forest or open, lightly wooded areas) 
Fc : Restricted to coastal or island forests 
G : Grasslands 
O : Open and disturbed areas (grassland, urban, agricultural, scrub 
etc.) 
Oc : Open areas near the coast 
S : Coastal or pelagic (oceanic) seabirds 
W : Wetland species: rivers, estuaries, lakes, marshes, etc. 
W/G : Wetlands and grasslands 
Wc :Coastal wetland species: mangroves, estuaries, etc.
